RATING: 9
A game of set rounds set in the city of Troyes and utilizing a very unique dice action selection mechanism.

Each round you gather a die (the die have different colours and can be used for different regions of the board, religious, civil, and military) for each person you have in one of 3 different cities and roll them.  These dice will be used to perform several different actions: make money, add presence to the cathedral (scores points and influence), fight the marauders (scores points and influence), add more people to cities (therefore get more dice and thus more actions later), add people to building cards (scores end game points and allows access to a wide variety of abilities).  There are a couple of interesting twists with the dice selection: you can purchase other people's die, thereby taking their actions for your own in essence, you can also re-roll or "flip" bad rolls into good ones paying with influence (another currency effectively).  Therefore you can win with lots of dice or little, with "bad" rolls or good ones.  Money is tight, so passing is another option and you generate income this way similar in fashion to the Amyitis.

Each round you also have to as a group, fight the marauders to a limited extent and quite likely weather the storm of their attacks (some negative penalties)- it therefore gives incentive to the action of fighting the marauders.  Each player is secretly assigned an end game point-scoring character card (based on end game conditions) that all players can potentially score for so you need to keep an eye on what other players are focusing on.  

This game scales very well from 2-4 players and has alot of moving parts and is truly a strategy gamer's game.

Own the [boardgame=94284]bonus cards[/boardgame] and the [boardgame=127061]Ladies of Troyes[/boardgame] expansion.  The Ladies of Troyes expansion adds the following (which can be modular if you wish): a purple die for each player which cannot be purchased and which can be used as ANY colour, there are 3 new end game persons, there is a variant rule for adding an additional person face up for all players to be able to target for scoring, there are several new building and marauding cards, and there are the outdoor activities.  The outdoor activities allow for a new action which is the movement of your tower guard around the castle and when he reaches an activity, he has access to it which provides abilities and/or scoring opportunities- this action is very useful and powerful and should not be overlooked.  The expansion does not contain alot of materials but it increases the variability and replayability of an already great game so I definitely recommend it.

RATING: 8
3 plays / 3,4 players

LIKE: The interaction of buying and manipulation of the dice is intricate and satisfying. Players can plan to use dice for a big move, or to protect their meeple from being pushed out. The push and pull of the dice resources is a bit conflicting, but not terrible, since players can buy other player's dice. I also enjoyed the secret, yet shared, end game goals, because it's fun to try to guess what each player has.

DISLIKE: High price tag, not much physical content in the box. The dice can be random and work against players. Lots of little rules for each of the events, but not a hinderance.

OVERALL: Very good game because of the interactivity. The game can have a very slight meanness to it, but this is very minor and keeps the game interesting.

Muchos dados que se adquieren con meeples colocados, que lanzan y dejan en reserva cada jugador en centro tablero. Quizá las cartas de evento esté un poco OP de PV que otorgan. Duración contenida, si bien al principio puede generar mucho AP y el tema de la "división" a primerizos les cuesta. 
En general, muy buen euro, que no se parece tanto a otros, chapeau!

RATING: 9.5
[i]2017/04/28 - 2 plays 2-4p[/i]

Outstanding engine building and dice management game.

A lot of depth with a limited number of decisions. As a result, the game plays fast if no one suffers from analysis paralysis.

Replayability is through the roof, and the wonderful expansion Ladies of Troyes, while absolutely not needed, brings even more goodness.

The game is very interactive, with unusually direct boot kicking for this type of euro. Nevertheless, no take that per se.

The art is original and I love it, but not everybody will. Theme is thin, this is a mechanics first game, with a nice arc from early, mid and end-game. As a result, theme-first gamers may find Troyes quite dry.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Very interested in playing this more to fully explore the design. Won my initial game at BGG.CON (a squeaker!) and absolutely love the decisions surrounding the use of dice. It seems best to think of dice not as your own in the sense that you are going to get to use them, but rather as potential, either for cheap dice placement or for gold when people pay you to use them. 

Ladies is an interesting expansion, I didn't think I was actually going to like the purple die as it seemed like it would rob the game of some tension, but it actually allows for some measure of interesting decisions, and all the additional cards are great (e.g. I think trebuchet is a much more balanced card than archers). Doubling the potential scoring categories makes for a much more tense finish, some in the group have expressed that it makes it feel a little too much like a crapshoot, and I can agree to some degree, but it also means you REALLY need to pay attention to what everyone else is doing.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I'm not sure what it is about Troyes that bugs me.  It does a lot of things right, has innovative mechanics, and plays in a reasonable amount of time.  At the same time, it can be somewhat random (certain remaining dice -will- get all your workers bumped off their buildings, and then you burn resources and other awkwardness while you try and recover) and the iconography is a disaster.  Worse, it is often surprisingly unintuitive and surprisingly fiddly.  While I'll play it sometimes, the lack of a theme and the "meh"-ness of the game as a whole put it pretty far down the list.

RATING: 7
2-4(3)

You know, I liked it, but our first play felt awfully long, with eight rounds and 18 dice to spend. There's an interesting bit of gamesmanship in trying to determine your opponent's scoring card. But really, the core of the game is in the dice and the use of them. It's not really more complex than Kingsburg, but it feels a lot more meaningful. There are a lot of options here, with battling events, trade cards, principal buildings, etc., as opposed to Kingsburg, in which your options are pretty well constrained by your dice and there is often a "clear" "best option". Troyes will have some AP for this, which extends the game, but I think otherwise it's well-balanced, with some mechanisms (namely, buying dice) that really bring something to the table. Also, there is a downside in combo (or trade) spamming, in which a single trade card will get throttled all game, essentially eliminating the variability from the other trade cards.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
There's a lot going on in Troyes.  Combine that with a new set of icons to learn and a fresh twist of the allocate dice to do stuff genre and there's a bit of a learning curve.  Get beyond that and you'll find a game that challenges you to make the best use of three resources -  dice, money and influence - and gives you a variable set of tools (the various profession cards) to play with.  My one concern is that some combinations are very powerful.  The last game I played was a first encounter with the Monk + Sculptor juggernaut.  The end result wasn't close. 

I rather like the Medieval-style artwork.  The production quality is excellent.

RATING: 8.4
I rate this highly because it has many paths to victory, varied options as to what you can do on your turn and a unique triggering mechanic, all pluses.

At-start, place your five meeples on one of three buildings, which are color coded.  Gain dice  (also color coded) corresponding to the buildings your meeples occupy to activate special abilities of cards or fight events.

However, you aren't confined to using your own dice.  At a monetary cost, you can purchase the dice of the other players; so, the game has a nice screwage factor, which is another plus.  In addition, dice can be used to bump other players off the various tracks, blocking their progress in relation to the Activity Cards in those areas.

You must also manage an influence track, influence enabling players to place meeples from their supply, re-roll or simply change die values.

The event cards also trigger the game's attacking of the players, so attention must be paid to what the game is going to do to thwart your plans, as well.

RATING: 7
A neat game which uses dice and cards but feels more strategic than random. In a way it feels like a suped-up version of Stone Age where you use dice to activate various areas (rolling for resources in Stone Age), and depending on the value of the dice determines the amount of times you activate the card's action. Despite these problems still a lot of fun!

RATING: 5
Troyes has some cool ideas, like pushing other players' workers off buildings and collectively fighting the events with black dice.  I also like the artwork, although the symbols that explain what the action cards do are not very easy to understand.

What I'm really, really confused about is why the designers of Troyes chose to use dice in their game.  What's the point of rolling dice if you can easily purchase better dice from your opponents or manipulate your own?  I feel like half the rules are just there to offset the randomness of the dice, and it really defeats the "make the best of what you have" mentality that make other dice games, like Kingsburg, work.

RATING: 8.5
¿Un juego de dados donde casi no hay suerte?. Esto hay que probarlo. Troyes es un juego estupendo, terriblemente elegante en sus mecánicas y con un arte en tableros y cartas muy inmersivo. 
Cada partida se juegan con 9 cartas de tres mazos distintos elegidas ala azar de entre otras muchas al inicio de la partida. Eso otorga  durabilidad al juego. 
La idea es buscar la interacción entre estas cartas para poder usarlas correctamente y sacar el máximo beneficio  al combo. Cada jugador intentará hacer lo mismo y de hecho habrá un par o tres combinaciones buenas así que el que mejor se adapte al devenir del juego será el vencedor.

Las reglas, una vez captada la esencia, son muy sencillas y se puede introducir a no jugones empezando a jugar diractamente, sin explicación previa. Una vez visto el primer turno las mecánicas son tan sencillas que lo que queda es saber jugar con las cartas y el puteo que puedas ocasionar (se dice pronto, pero eso es otro cantar).

Tendría que jugarlo más. Con esta frase ya te lo digo todo.

RATING: 8
Place meeples to decide what dice you'll get, roll the dice to decide what actions will be available to you, use money or influence to mitigate the luck of the roll by buying dice from other players or changing your own. Player interaction is high.

It is a tactical game: what you do each turn depends on your rolls, and on the event and action cards that get turned up. The cards make for interesting game variability but also means it is slow to teach – you'll have to explain to newbies all cards each time they come up, and it is not always easy to appreciate their worth.

I've played with two, three and four players, it works well at all counts. The cards, board and dice look lovely.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The best debut game from a company I can recall.  The best deep (gamer’s game) dice game I can think of, even ahead of Yspahan.  The choice of which dice to use is tense, knowing the ones you don’t chose will probably be gone by the time you have another turn.  The cost of using your opponents’ dice is well pitched.  The adequate income means you can survive with few dice.  Alternatively invest in more dice by bringing in new workers, but forgo current actions.  The restriction on expelling existing workers - not more than one of an opponent's from each location, is also pitched well, as is the method with either direct replacement or a queuing system depending on the location.  There are so many other nice and original ideas it was a delight to play.  Probably my favourite and the most original Essen 2010 game, even ahead of Civilisation, 7 Wonders and Key Market.  Looking forward to further plays. Recommended.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Először is, örülök, hogy végre kipróbálhattam, az idei esseni kínálatból a top3 várományosaim egyike volt ez a játék (a 7 wonders és a sid meiers civ. mellett). Nem csalódtam, pedig magam sem tudom, miért vártam ennyire ezt a játékot. Mondhatjuk, hogy pusztán a képek alapján. Nagyon szépnek tűnt a tábla, a doboz... A játék során vita alakult ki, 2:2 arányban tartottuk szépnek ill. csúnyának a játékot. A legszebbek szerintem a kártyák, így amikor már mind a 9 akciókártya fenn van, akkor a legszebb a játék.

Na de rátérve a lényegre: A 90 perces játikidő egy vicc. Tisztán 3 óra volt a játék, és állítólag ez nem egyedi eset, hanem általános. Ami nekem alapból nem tetszett, az lehet, hogy szőrszálhasogatás, de 4 személy esetén 2 ember 2-szer kezd, a másik kettő meg csak egyszer. Az épületekben a helyért fizetni kell, így végső soron nem túl nagy előny, amihez jut valaki azáltal, hogy több kockával dobhat. Ha csak 1 kockát akarok használni az akciómban, gyakran ugyanannyiért vehetem meg, mint amennyit a tulajdonosa a dobásért fizetett (ráadásul ő akciót is áldozott rá). Szóval szerintem túl van kompenzálva a dobás lehetőségének elvesztése. Ami még nem tetszik, és lassan a végére is érek a negatívumoknak, hogy a végső 6 bónusz-szerzési lehetőségből elég lutri, hogy melyikek szerepelnek a játékban. Oké, lehet sejteni abból, hogy ki mire gyúr, de mégis, túl sok múlik ezen, ahhoz, hogy így derüljön ki. Ja még egy furcsaság: van egy mező a játékban, ahol pénzt lehet szerezni akcióként: ezt senki sem használta. Volt jobb mező. Talán van olyan eset, amikor szükség lehet rá, de még nem találkoztam olyannal, aki használta. Ez azért elég nagy tesztelési hiba.

A játékban szerintem egyébként nagyon nagy újrajátszhatósági lehetőségek rejlenek. Elég kreatívan nyúltak a kockás témához, teljesen másra jó a fehér kocka, mint a piros, talán kicsit gyengének érzem azt a funkciót, amivel embert helyezhetünk el az épületekben, a fentebb írtak miatt (könnyű pótolni a kimaradó kockadobásokat). A presztízspontok is tetszenek. Elég konfrontatív a játék, de számomra ez belefér. A játék nem vészesen komplex, pont az élvezhetőség határán mozog. Nem annyira egzakt, mint mondjuk a Caylus, nem lesz a top10-emben, de elég eredeti, szívesen játszanék vele bármikor!!!

Még egy megjegyzés: ez inkább taktikai, mint stratégiai játék, bár kell egy vezérfonal, de mivel nincs biztonságban a kockánk a többiektől, ezért a pillanatnyi helyzet dönt.
---
Másodjára játszva én készültem a szabályból: bonyolultabbra emlékeztem:) Meglepően rövid, egyszerű és letisztult, nincs sok kivétel. A játék komplexitásának java részét a kártyák adják szerintem, azok nincsenek a szabályban, külön lapra kerültek.
---
A harmadik játék után kicsit rontottam a pontszámon. Minden oké a játékkal, de azért nem ad pl. Macao, Notre Dame vagy Pillars of the Earth szintű élményt... Márpedig eddig ugyanannyi ponton állt, mint a felsoroltak.

RATING: 7
I can see the gears of this game turning a bit too clearly, which is usually not a problem for me in eurogames. There are a lot of calculations involved; moves that precariously depend on having exactly the right amount of resources available.

Usually mechanics wound up this tightly do not feature dice! Overlooking some minor detail is extremely easy, and can cost you dearly. The game does get better the more you play, but I still can't entirely shake off the feeling of Troyes being just one big math homework exercise.

Worker placement with dice. Although this is the same mechanic already seen in Kingsburg, this is a whole next level in this genre.
There's three different colours of dice, each with its own characteristics, and every color is absolutely different from others.
There's different action cards every game, and that's added value for replayability.
Every dice can be modified, and every player can even use dice from other players!
The first game has been completely opaque, in the sense that no one understood more than a small percentage of what he did... but under the opacity I saw the glimpse of a masterpiece!

RATING: 9.5
Like Ginkgopolis, this is a game that takes a number of mechanisms and mixes them in an interesting way. The dice rolling is satisfying and the action selection/worker placement hybrid mechanism is quite good. Once you get used to the game, you'll have a fun game that plays smoothly even with people that agonize over their dice selection.

The base game has great replayability despite being fairly limited in the scope of its actions. It was quite surprising to find how differently each game plays. The replayability is largely due to the action and event cards that get turned over throughout the game. The game even provides a hint of narrative through those aforementioned cards. 

For some negatives, it can play a bit longer as you learn the game. I've found the rulebook to be overly condensed, which makes learning the game a bit more difficult than other rulebooks of games with a similar weight. The first few games are slowed further due to the iconography being non-intuitive. This game should have just put the text on the cards instead of going for language independence (though its aesthetic would have been impacted). Finally, there are mechanisms to mitigate a bad dice rolls, but a player that has multiple rounds of truly bad rolls will have an uphill battle staying competitive.

Edit, Sept 2017: I have incorporated the event cards of Ladies into Troyes. First off, the cards do not match. The backs of the red action cards are noticeably different. The differences in the other cards are less noticeable. That said, the action cards and purple dice add a lot of good stuff to the game. However, adding in all the new action cards highlight how AWFUL the iconography is on the cards. You'll want to print out the action/even card player aide listed in the files section of the Ladies expansion. The cost-to-production ratio is definitely off for the base game and especially for the Ladies expansion. The game, however, is still special, so I'm not reducing its rating despite the publisher's bad decisions.

I don't have any plan on using the ramparts module any time soon. It seems superfluous.

Promos/Extras/Expansions:
Troyes: Bonus Cards (2011) 
The Ladies of Troyes (2012)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[i]Posted: 7/10/112, Rated: 7[/i]

The experience of learning a new game greatly influences the likelihood that you will want to return to it.  Returning to a game grants you additional perspective to judge it.  I learned [i]Troyes[/i] at BGG.con '11, and was taught by a long time Internet friend and fellow Werweolf player seanp.  I [i]wanted[/i] to like the game.

The theme and artwork are very pleasing: I think the game looks [i]good.[/i]  Dealing role cards to the different buildings enforces variation between games, and thus provides replayability: there is no scripted opening, here.  Being able to modify dice values, and to purchase dice from other players results in many player options.  Lastly, variable and hidden bonus conditions make it more difficult to evaluate those options.

The result is that [i]Troyes[/i] turns out to be very "thinky" for a game that uses dice as a resource.  It is difficult to plan ahead for your turn, as the dice you want to buy may not be available by the time you're ready to play.  The games moves more slowly than it should.  This feeling may be mitigated when playing with experienced players.

Dice manipulation games have seen a resurgence.  After having played many of them including [i][thing=43249]The BoardGameGeek Game[/thing][/i], [i][thing=55670]Macao[/thing][/i], and [i][thing=84876]The Castles of Burgundy[/thing][/i], I can say that [i]Troyes[/i] is the heaviest.  If you expect dice games to be light, then you will be unhappy.  If you expect medium weight games to eschew dice, then you will also be unhappy.  When played with the right group, it is an enjoyable time even if it does feel a little long for what it is, but then again that could be said of any game.  An average sentiment earns an average rating.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Pro's:

1) The art, aesthetics and historical theme are particularly fantastic, for me the game does feel thematic even though the game elements will sometimes override the theme.
2) Randomization of the cards and events make each game slightly different.
3) Figuring out the best combinations of cards and how to maximize your dice usage feels very strategically deep.
4) Events forcing the players to battle some of the issues in common to prevent them from getting out of hand while also maintaining the competitive player element is one of my favourite aspects. 
5) There are many ways to mitigate dice rolls. One of the few games where it feels great to roll 1's since you can always mitigate them. The ability to purchase dice means you are nearly always able to do what you want to do with the game occasionally pushing you into a certain direction.

Con's:

1) I wish there were a few more cards of each type.
2) Some of the iconography is not entirely clear and the rulebook needs a few more clarifications.

RATING: 6
This game is...interesting. 

I appreciate a lot about this game. Every game plays a bit differently. The puzzles it presents always change depending on variable cards and the other player's strategies. There's a lot of little mechanisms that interweave nicely with each other, and there doesn't seem to be a clear way for anyone to win in any particular game. It's also quite interactive with other players, which is something I need to have in any game I buy...

But for some reason I still come away from the game thinking "Meh, that was okay.", and Im not sure why. My best guess is that it requires a decent amount of thought, but I'm not really rewarded for the mental gymnastics as much as I think I should be? Also, there's no way I'd be able to teach this to most people - this is a gamer's game in complexity - and I think that issue in accessibility makes me a little bit sad.

Sold after a couple lackluster plays with friends

RATING: 6.5
OK, so I wasn't really in the mood and, whilst I like dice games, I hate the ones where rolling high is good and rolling low is bad.

So take the following with a pinch of salt.

Setup was fiddly and rules were very complex for what is quite a simple game. Seemed like there were about twice as many things to do and keep track of than the game really needed.

Some of the concepts are nice - only knowing a bit about what the final win bonuses will be, having to co-operate to destroy the black cards (because if you let them multiply you all suffer). However, the artwork and symbols are almost wilfully non-intuitive and having to remember the possible win bonuses is no joke.

Probably one that you would enjoy lots if you played it lots, but I am not sure I want to make the investment.

RATING: 7.5
There are a lot of good things to say about Troyes. The traditional artwork enveloped by the muted tones of the overall board is quite appealing and original within the genre. The overall concept of the game leans slightly heavy, giving players a plethora of choices bounded within a dice-drafting, worker placement fist fight. 

However, as play progresses, the game takes a weird turn. Sometimes, there's not enough money; sometimes the variable card-setup just isn't that exciting. For me, the choices were not as captivating even though the theme was hilarious. "Oh look, a marauding brigand of monks engaged in theological conflict..." (The Table Erupts)... so I'll use these dice to put cubes on some of these events.... zzzz

So far, its kind of a snooze.

Sébastien Dujardin

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Es un juego de dados en el que los dados no son especialmente determinantes y sirve para muchas cosas. Cada jugador tiene un distrito y comienza con 4 meeples en el tablero. Su ubicación determina cuantos dados y de qué color va a tirar. Una vez que todos han tirado los dados, estos pueden utilizarse para activar cartas, que generan diversas ventajas, echar a otros meeples de  su sito (y conseguir más dados en turnos siguietnes), luchar contra los enemigos, etc, etc. Son demasiadas cosas. El juego es dificil de explicar, pero una vez que se entiende, es sencillo. Puedes comprar los dados a tus contrincantes y usarlos para tus propósitos (no se pueden negar a la venta). Durante el juego se van sumando puntos de victoria que permanecen ocultos y al final hay un recuento y se incrementan según los personajes que entren en juego. Hay tres categorías de cartas: clero, militar y civil. De cada una de ellas hay 9 pero sólo se usan 3 al azar en cada partida y cada una representa una actividad que genera ventajas, por lo que la rejugabilidad es bestial. Una auténtica joya. Le he cascado un 8.5 en mi primera partida, y tiene pinta de convertirse en otro caylus. Muy, muy recomendable, aunque dificil de jugar con no-jugones.

Añadido a mi colección en Mayo de 2011. En principio era un pedido conjunto con Marc, pero cuando fui a pagarselo no me cogió el dinero y me dijo que era un regalo. Esto le ha convertido en una de las piezas más valiosas de mi ludoteca.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 1
It's a highly tactical point salad with a mildly innovative core mechanism. Game feels pretty scripted - get money early, convert X resource into big points in Ages 3/4/5, while building up to score your end game bonus card. Great, I can do the same thing 4-5 times in half the length with Race for the Galaxy.

I love interactivity in games, but this is the type of interaction I hate. There's no way to make long term plans. Your opponent does a move that screws up what you were about to do, and you do the same back to them. It's an aimless back and forth, rather than an interesting build up into a devastating move.

For a mid-weight Euro, try something like Concordia that's far more cohesive a design, has much more meaningful interaction, and gives players agency in what they want to do.

RATING: 6.5
Another solid BGG top 100 Euro. Interesting twist on worker placement in that your workers stay on the board and can be re-used round to round, but can be "pushed off" their square by someone else. The extra card locations seemed to be a bit of a grab bag, and once everyone latched on to the strategy of defeating enemy cards for points, that seemed to be the clear path to go, calling into doubt the value of some other tactics. I'm sure with multiple plays other more legitimate scoring paths present themselves, but on the first play effective strategic options seemed limited. 

Glad I played it, but unlike some other gems I've found on this quest, not in a huge hurry to replay.

RATING: 8
-sold-

sleeved

As soon as someone recommends Alien Frontiers, Voyages of Marco Polo or Kingsburg you hear the whispers: "Troyes did it first and did it best."

Unfortunately it is OOP and expensive. Luckily I got a copy and had the chance to try it.

I understand why it is the ancestor of these games, although it plays very differently.
-events are a small semi-co-op-element where you have to decide how much you sacrifice for the group for just a small benifit.
-dice aren't taken for granted, you have to plan which dice you want to have next round or you have to pay for them

Currently it shares the #2 with Alien Frontiers right behind Marco Polo. Time will tell.

RATING: 7
Tentative 7 after first play.  A pretty enjoyable game.  It's nice to play a euro that feels innovative, and this certainly a nice twist from the standard "place workers, get crap, build crap".   It's certainly never trivial working out what the best move is.

The stealing of dice seems a little harsh to me, as it effectively steals potentially valuable actions away from players for little compensation.  

I'm a little hesitant of games where a player in the lead can get pounded into submission (losing the game directly because you played a strong early game just feels wrong to me), and in a 4 player game of Troyes, you could potentially have most of your dice stolen by the other 3 players before you ever got to do anything, which feels a little punitive.

RATING: 7
11/20/2012 - This is a very solidly designed game. I had it for a year before playing it, because I feared it was too dry and "euro" for me. While it is very euro, it has enough dice action and player interaction to give it some soul. It feels a bit like Alien Frontiers, since it uses dice as workers for worker placement. But there are MANY more strategic and tactical options in Troyes than in Alien Frontiers. That said, I don't like the Troyes theme as much, the graphical design is jarring and ugly, and the higher learning curve means I won't get it to the table as much as I do Alien Frontiers. Those positives and negatives mostly offest each other, so I'm rating Troyes an "8" for now -- the same as my rating for Alien Frontiers.

:thumbsup: [b]POSITIVE POINTS:[/b]

++ Great medium-heavy-weight dice-drafting / dice worker placement strategy game with many paths to victory, which unveil as the game progress, so players must be strategic and tactical.
++ I like that the game have "Event" that are randomly took from a deck of card at each round that may, or may not, affect all players' short-term strategy and that everyone most work on, kind of semi-cooperatively.
++ The fact that you can buy opponents' dice and that you can also manipulate your dices (flip them or re-rolled them using "Influence" points) is a great way to mitigate the luck of the dice rolls and also adds up  a layer of strategy.
++ Simple but effective dummy-player mechanism.
++ Good replayability due to only 3 out of 9 "Activity" cards (for each color) used per game, especially if you can get the bonus cards.
++ There is one expansion available for this game (which is useful to refresh the game when we have overplayed it).
++ The Ladies of Troyes expansion really add a lot of "more of the same": more Activity cards, more Event cards, more Character cards, etc. This bring in a lot of variability that is really welcome when we have played the game a lot. We really enjoyed this added variety and the new combo possible with all the new cards.


:thumbsdown: [b]NEGATIVE POINTS:[/b]

-- The art style is hit or miss with people and thus the aesthetic can turn off some people.
-- Harder and longer to teach to new people.

RATING: 8

I also love the event cards that occur turn by turn that punish the players, a la the events in Year of the Dragon.  I always love games that punish the players, so this is one of my favorite elements in a game.  It's also cool that these events will recur until the players finally defeat the event to remove it from the game.

Overall, a very cool strategy game with a little dose of luck added with the dice.  Good stuff!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Troyes gets the nod here because it seems to be the one recent purchase that I find myself most wanting to play. I'm just not sure how long it will continue to captivate me. I'm impressed with both the production and the design, but the game isn't one I'm likely to obsess over for months. The last play felt a little easy. Perhaps, like Hansa Teutonica, it will depend on the abilities of experienced players to up the ante of an otherwise straightforward game? 

Troyes is a resource management game, sure, but it's not a game that involves early investment in position/resources followed by a wholesale transition into VP production. Nearly every action you take provides small VP gains and very few provide large ones. I think of it as more of a game of tactical opportunism. I usually try to open the game defensively, establishing positions that keep me from being dependent on particular colors of dice. I also like to have a decent source of income. After that, I simply look for good scoring and screwage opportunties on a round-by-round basis and try to deduce which characters are in play so that I don't fall too far behind on any one axis. 

Mind you, I've only played two-player and the two-player game is only four rounds long, so the notion of a long-term strategy is a little silly -- you would be comitting to an approach before knowing what opportunities are available. Perhaps this is why the game also feels a bit light to me at present: I'm not constantly adding to a single, coherant VP engine; I'm simply positioning myself to have multiple opportunities for small-scale card synergies depending on the needs of the moment.

On this note, I suspect that the primary drawbacks to the game are exacerbated in the two-player game. The game still feels too tactical, even now that we are familiar with the card functions. This would likely be mitigated with each additional round of exposed Activity Cards (in the two-player game, a new card is revealed in 3 rounds out of 4). The dice-purchasing dynamics are also diluted in the two-player game (admittedly, this could be a groupthink issue). Each player, including the neutral player, has so many dice to begin the round that everyone has a little of everything and the need to purchase from your opponent is rarer than I expected it to be. 

Some games become noticeably better with the addition of a third player -- Brass, Agricola, and Neuland come immediately to mind. I can't tell whether that will be the case with Troyes (which is still good with just two), but I can tie enough of my complaints to features more prominent in the two-player game that I will hold off on officially rating it until I get it to the table with more. Will AP and downtime be a countervailing consideration with increased player counts?

A second complaint that has nothing to do with player count, and which will likely keep Troyes from the tippy-top of my ratings, is that the game feels very dry and procedural. There are too many little steps to do in a particular order, and none of them have an intuitive thematic guidepost that keeps me on track. I always just instinctively jump right to the dice-rolling and activity phases. It's something like this:

[q]
Me: *Roll* Awesome! I'll activate the Archer. 
Jenn: We need to do income first.
Me: Oh, yeah. Ok...um...2, 4, 6, 7...I get 3 money. Okay, I'll activate the Archer!
Jenn: We need to flip over event cards first.
Me: Oh, hehe. Yeah. So...red card, and this one says white. Okay. So that's 5 black dice, and we both lose 2 influence, and then remove a cube from the cathedral. Hmm...I guess I'll counter these two with this red die and take two influence. 
Jenn: I'll counter this one with this white. 
Me: Damn...umm...I guess I'll counter the last two with this other red die for two more influence. Okay, now I'll activate the Archer! 
Jenn: Actually, I'm first this turn. I'll buy your last red die for 2. 
Me: :soblue:[/q]

Still, if you're the sort of gamer who enjoyed Homesteaders, I recommend checking Troyes out. 

If I had a 7.5 rating, [thing=73439][/thing] would have a comfortable home.  I'm impressed with the design and the production, but I don't find it gripping.  It's a little fiddly, a little procedural, and a little AP-inducing.  Despite all that, it also doesn't quite feel quite complex enough.  I would put it closer to Hansa Teutonica than to Le Havre in terms of weight.  Would like to see how it goes with 3 players. 


1/24/11: This weekend we finally taught the game to a third player. What a difference! The 3-player game was head and shoulders above anything we had experienced with just two. It just seemed to come to life. While it still had some flow issues, it was certainly more dynamic and interesting. In particular, repelling the event cards and purchasing dice seemed much more lively when the results weren't zero sum. Additionally, having only three character cards in play made that whole guessing game a little more exiting. 

Ultimately, I don't think the game's going to drastically change in my esteem, but it certainly seems much more promising than it did only a week ago. Could it potentially reach the tail end of my 8s?

RATING: 8
Great dice placement game where players activate various board actions through their dice or opponents' dice. Very nice twist on the usual dice placement which makes prioritizing dice very important before other players nab your good dice as their own. I really did enjoy how the board grows for the first 3 rounds by adding an extra action to each on the 3 main buildings. I also enjoy that there are a few different possible cards for each action spot which makes each game very different. I wish the artwork was a bit more colorful and I think choosing pink to represent "any color" is a bit too close to red. But these are just a few nitpicks for a really enjoyable game for dice and worker placement enthusiasts.

RATING: 8
This game is one of Rahdo's top 10 of all time. Shut Up & Sit Down also highly recommend this game. 

Patricia doesn't like dice, and the game is too heavy for the group. So... a must-buy? :)

It has been out-of-print since forever, but I got one of the reprints.

After a disastrous first play (four-player game), I wasn't sure if this game was worth all the hype. The rules were hard for some players to understand. Some people thought that buying the dice was "too mean." It turned into a three-hour slog.

We played a two-player game after that. The game was quick - only 90 minutes. It went fast, there was still competition, and we were better at guessing one another's character cards. It ended up in tie 45 points each. (I realized after the game that I could have scored *one more point* and won the game.)

I think the game will work, once everyone is used to the game. There is a slight learning curve, though.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Troyes is a great dice game, probably only second to Castles of Burgundy! The reason why these games succeed in my book is that the luck of the dice is greatly reduced! In CoB even more so perhaps, as higher numbers are probably better in Troyes, but there are lots of options to change and manipulate your dice rolls which is awesome. Lower numbers might actually be quite good too, especially in the end, as the other players won’t buy your dice, while you can still buy their high dice all while also changing your own low dice to higher numbered dice if you’ve got the influence. This influence mechanic was integrated very well and I liked how you need to balance money, influence and victory points!

There are a lot of very good mechanics; how you fight against the black cards, how you push other players’ meeples out of the buildings and thereby getting extra dice yourself, how you buy dice from your opponents, how you pass and can get extra money etc. One thing I liked a lot was the secret objectives, but I don’t think they are going to have a big impact on the pointscoring; I would guess everybody always gets at least 3 points and probably the full 6 (if I remember correctly). The variety because of the fact that only 9 cards are used in each game means that the game has great replayability.

Troyes was much simpler than I had thought and heard everybody say. The rules really aren’t that difficult. Personally I thought the artwork is ugly, but I don’t really care. I am looking forward to the next time Troyes is getting on the table!

RATING: 5
Rated 6 after one play, but this could go all over the place depending on reactions to future plays.

For a first game of Troyes, I spent half the game trying to simply hold it together and learn the flow of a turn. With workers, and dice, and dice allocation, and money, and influence... it's a lot to hold together. I couldn't even really bother to try a strategy; I was just trying to survive and accomplish basic "point mongering."

On the one hand, there are some clever mechanics, and it's an incredibly big "playground" for trying different moves. On the other hand, there's almost no theme. I had no idea what cards represented, but it didn't really seem to matter. I started catching a spark at the end of the game, though, so I need to play this again to see if I discover more to enjoy.

Update: 2nd play, and I like it a little more. The game required more than one play for me to assess. It has very clever mechanics, and the use of dice is a strong feature. Still, I see almost no theme, so I feel like I'm just point grabbing. Also, there's a shocking diverse of options on any given turn. I normally like games that provide decision angst, and not "decision flooding." It's a solid game, I'll play it, but I won't go clamoring for it.

I typically love dice drafting and love Grand Austria Hotel, but I think the theme in there pulls me in so much more than this. I would definitely play this again and could see my rating go either way with a second play, but with it being a little heavier and a few more fiddly rules I think I'd always reach for GAH first.

:sugar: :( :) :D :) Finally, a designer with the courage to really stretch the boundaries of Euro-game subject matter: building a medieval city in France! Taking on the role of a prominent family in the city and competing with others to win glory through great monuments! Converting in-game commodities to VPs by patronizing the local barleywright or duckmonger! Building a cathedral, as you do! Brown-nosing some distant unpleasable monarch! This is some hot buttered innovation right here, faithful readers.

So yeah.

And many mechanical aspects, in turn, will also put one in mind of Extruded Euro Product #24 if you're used to it. Interaction is mostly light and indirect via blocking snatching and denying. Theme is both unoriginal and insubstantial. Gameplay is puzzly, with much pondering how you can most efficiently use the resources you have at hand in a given turn. Long-term strategy is difficult to plot out as available actions shift from turn to turn based on the fall of the dice -- while strategy is very much not non-existent, short-term tactics are a big part of the game.

You have, in significant ways, seen this film before, and if you're not a crazed devotee who desperately needs to own every Euro depicting city-building and resource-trading from late antiquity to early-modern Europe, you might be forgiven for giving this a miss. (And if you are, may God have mercy on your bank account, for CoolStuffInc will not.)

In many ways, then, this is a game tailor-made for indifference or dislike on my part. [i]And yet[/i], at first blush at least, I find myself thinking, well, damn: Stone Age and Kingsburg had a baby and it's a much better'n mom [i]or[/i] dad.

The game presents an array of options that a) change from game to game (part of what inhibits early long-term planning is not knowing what's on deck in terms of buildings once Turn 3 rolls around) b) force a substantial but not brain-fryingly difficult decision on every play and c) are not so samey as to descend into pure VP salad where everything you do yields goodies.

Hidden victory goals are a nice example of information asymmetry done right: you know one of the scoring criteria and over the course of the game can intuit the others, in part because the designers made the laudible decision to constrain the list of possible VP conditions to a manageable list of six, and in part because someone else will usually provide a useful "tell" by the priorities they show by their actions.

Likewise the number of possible action spaces per round per dice color is large enough as to encourage strategic flexibility without being so large as to leave victory in the hands of fate and/or the whims of the deck.

In short, I think that expanding these sorta-random aspects of the game (which would have been easy to do at a small marginal increase in cost), while it would have superficially increased the "replayability" of Troyes, would actually possibly result in a worse play experience.

Of course, this is just a guess, based on one (1) play so far. Further trials will tell the tale.

One aspect of the game -- the key aspect in some ways -- that seems like it introduces a bit element of chance (the dice, natch) actually ends up being a lot more controllable than you'd think at first. This game doles out prestige like fast-food joints give out ketchup packets, and intelligent use of your stock of prestige is what makes the dice work for you.

Finally, I must offer up a bit of praise for one superficial aspect of the game: I love the faux-medieval art and the presentation of the game in general. I like the use of good old meeples, and the chunky, tactile experience of the wooden bits and the dice. It wouldn't make a huge difference if the artwork more closely resembled the idealized/sanitized look of, say, Village, but something about the particular look/feel/color palette of this game draws me in.

A very pleasant experience, and a worthy choice for a medium-weight Euro with substance for a weeknight Meetup.

RATING: 8
Sold

Dice allocation game with hidden roles. Reasonable player interaction, without being directly adversarial. The luck of the dice is balanced by the ability to buy other dice from the other players. So unless you are first player and do an awesome roll (and therefore someone can't buy key dice before you use them) the luck element is significantly reduced. The money then acts as a further rebalancing/catchup mechanism throughout the game (i.e. if you lose your dice to other players they have to pay you for it). 

Each game will be different due to the different combination of activity and event cards, which means the game has good replayability, and leads to different strategies each game. 

In my first game I found it difficult to keep an accurate idea of the relative scoring positions of the players, and I thought I was dead last. I had however underestimated the VPs I had been accumulating in game relative to the others and ended up being equal first in a close scoring game.

RATING: 4
(Played at Spiel 2010.) This game caught my attention (and that of many others) because of the claim of an innovative dice mechanism. Indeed, it is innovative: one allocates a number of dice in different colours to players, with the colours dependent on where the players have put some of their meeples, and then groups them together in the centre of the board. However it remains clear which player threw what dice. Then beginning with the starting player groups of dice are chosen to various things with them. A player can select his own dice for free, and has to pay handsomely for the use of dice of other players. The actions he can perform are, for example, installing new meeples to obtain more dice in the future, activating cards a certain amount of times depending on the total value of the dice group he assembled and the card he chose, or contributing to the construction of the cathedral. This goes on for several rounds, and at some point someone wins.

So what happens with low-valued dice? You can always use these to move in new meeples for more dice in the future, but since you tend to economise there ought to be a bias towards a greater churn at the lower end. A 6 is simply more useful elsewhere than a 1. You can also spend a little influence to flip over the die before using it, or spend some more to reroll. In this way there is no true ‘bad’ roll unless all of your throws are categorically low. The effect is that most of the time everything is useful and thus that the capriciousness of the dice is tamed.

On paper this looks pretty cool. In reality, I found the entire affair overwrought. What the cheerful stories [i]aren't[/i] telling you is that this game utilises three kinds of resources which more or less run together: money, influence, and VP. These three resources embark on a difficult conversion dance: for one action you pay money and gain influence; another costs you influence to gain VP; to use other players' dice you pay cash, but to turn over your own dice you use influence... It becomes a very mathy game where literally every single move you make carries a different cost, expressed in different units, making it hard to compare them. The fact that you want VP in the end makes it hard to seriously invest in these side denominations too. Undoubtedly one becomes more fluent with the manipulation as experience settles in; but for casual play Troyes is better not placed on the table, in my opinion. In fact, it is this calculating business which leads me to wonder why it is such a smash hit with boardgame fans. It is a surprisingly heavy game for something involving dice, and in my opinion one long abstract litany of addition, multiplication and division, with (very) weak 'thematics', without a clear growth mechanism, and without oodles of specialty cards—things which usually signal that the ‘average gamer’ is about to become a drooling and jonesing addict. Perhaps once every while there is room for a Troyes-like game too.

In summary: good and clever use of dice, without doubt, and thus a member of the Dice Done Right™ Club, but embedded in a game which to me feels like sticky syrup. Give me Yspahan anytime with or without the new cards: moves along at a much better pace, and preserves the simplicity and quickness of throwing dice throughout the game.

[b]Update[/b] Upon replaying the game I still consider it to be an example of cute mechanism first, and creative game play second. Games with dice selection mechanisms in them should strive to move more quickly than Troyes. My appreciation for the title has increased a little, but kitchen sink symptoms withhold me from pushing up the rate to a 5.

RATING: 9
I love dice games that are done well. A good dice game puts in place restrictions and forces you to consider what you can achieve with what is available. Troyes takes this to the next level, because it gets you to think about what you can achieve with your opponents dice. This game gives you so much control over the dice that there is almost no luck. It took me a while to get around to playing the Ladies of Troyes, and that expansion just completes the game. Not terribly bothered by the ramparts, but the additional roles and goals add that little bit of extra variety that really rounds out the game. The purple die increases the control a little as well, which is a welcome addition.

RATING: 9
Absolutely great game. Playing Troyes for the first time I sat there simply amazed and perfectly still, just taking it all in. Extremely good example of fun, yet deep, gaming goodness. 

Gushing aside ... Troyes is an immensely repayable, solid game with multiple ways to win, filled with subtle player interactions, depth, flow, balance, tense decisions and a perfect level of opportunities to mess with other players (for me, that's low to moderate). This is simply a brilliant game on so many levels. One of the better Euro-type games I own without question. Very worthy of a strong "8" rating, just shy of a "9".

Not as difficult to explain as one might think or have heard. After the first game it really clicks with most gamers.

My first purchase of 2013. After nine plays? Even better. My rating went up to a 9, and has stayed there after kore than 20 plays. I still can't find a win, but hell, this is a great game. I am obsessed with this game. I love to teach the game at conventions.

[b]I own the following expansion(s) for the game:[/b]

Troyes Bonus Cards expansion
The Ladies of Troyes.

A rare expansion that actually adds a total of five mechanics that are exciting and easily usable to one of my favourite Euro games. Highly recommended expansion. I play with the new action cards, new event cards, purple dice, and the new women in my games of Troyes. Will hold off on the wall track rules until I have a better understanding of the subtle strategies of the base game (I have yet to win, which makes how much I love this game all the more remarkable).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
A very good euro game. Yes, euro with lots of dice. However, rolling ones doesn't mean you're screwed, as it is possible to manipulate dice a lot. Therefore randomness is reduced to a level where it has little impact on the game. 

The main mechanic of the game - taking from 1 to 3 dice and then dividing the result by a certain amount - works really well and opens a path for maaaaany possibilites, although is not very intuitive and sounds weird at first. Same goes to buying dice from others(!) - the fact that the cost of a single dice depends on the total amount of dice you want to use... yep, sounds odd and may be hard to explain, but once you get to it, it is pretty simple. Overall this game is a bit harsh when it comes to explaining the rules. I recommend to send your friends a gameplay video so then it's a lot easier to describe all the minor rules.

For me, there are three aspects that make this game shine: negative interaction, a unique approach to dice and combos. Despite the fact that Troyes is an euro, there are possibilities to mess with other players - you can buy their dice! Yep, you can roll sixes and then watch how it's just being taken from you. Pretty standard in this game. Once you understand the fact that dice you roll is in fact not entirely yours it's easy to accept and not irritiating. I like that mechanic very much. Secondly, you can push enemy meeples away from the buildings, therefore reducing the amount of dice they'll roll in the next turn. That's A LOT interaction for a euro, believe me. Another thing is all that connections and possible combos between activity cards. There are so many ways of gaining money, victory points etc. All you need is to see the combinations... and lots of dice of course. 

Drawbacks? Well, the game is dry. That's it. I have nothing against its graphic design, in fact I like it quite a bit(however I understand those who find it pretty ugly), it matches the theme and is excellent in its own, medieval right. But during the game you don't actually care. It's a point salad. An abstract euro game. Marauders attacking the city are represented by black dice. You have to defeat them... guess what, using your dice, representing civil, military or religious forces. For that purpose red dice is twice as strong, because it represents an army fighting against them. You simply have to imagine all of that. That's not the minus of the game, as it's euro. It's pretty standard. Just don't expect it to be extremaly thematic.

A solid 8 from me. I think even 8,5. Only 2-player games so far and, well, if it's true that it works best for 3 players then I'm looking forward to play that variant.

EDIT: Bumping to 9. I love how quick the game is despite the fact that it offers so many possibilities. It never outstays its welcome. 

It is excellent online as well. I've been playing it a lot on boardgamearena recently.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8

RATING: 7.5
Very good dice allocation game with a bunch of other stuff mixed in. Hidden end-game scoring, similar to the Impact Events in Through the Ages. Also includes a bit of cooperation in battling off militants as a team. 

It doesn't feel like a dice game, as there is virtually no such thing as a "bad" roll. There are so many options to do with the dice: spend influence for rerolls, buy dice away from opponents, low and high pips both useful for various actions.

Strategy tip: Although they count double against enemies, the red dice are not always the best to use to fight off enemies. The game sort of pushes you towards using the red dice for this, but I think this is sometimes a mistake, as there are often much better uses for these red dice.

Lots of difficult decisions to be made here.

Oh, one more thing. Please stop calling this a worker placement game. This is in no way a worker placement game. If anything, it's anti-worker placement, as you *remove* dice from the board to conduct actions. Not that it really matters though. :)

RATING: 7
I've played Troyes quite a bit and always had a good time. The random setup and character cards makes for an absorbing and varied playing experience. 

However, I'm a big fan of dice-allocation games (having played a lot and designed a couple) and I think it's fair to say that this isn't one: The dice that each player rolls become a random selection of available resources that are available for any player to buy. Making it more of a resource management game. This makes for a very interesting mechanic but not one comparable to (for example) Alien Frontiers or Kingsburg.

It's not inherently dowm-timey but the fact that each turn is dependent upon the available dice (which is dependent on previous players' actions) means that it's not a good game to play with the AP prone.

Also, while it's not to everyone's taste, I love the artwork and theme.

RATING: 7
Solid dice + action selection game. The interaction between the cards, dice, influence, money and scoring is quite elegant. There are lots of tough and interesting decisions to make. Compare that to most other eurogames, where the decisions are either horribly obvious and/or the interaction just isn't as well integrated.

The ability to influence or purchase dice effectively mitigates most bad die rolling. It's definitely much more about strategic placement than just rolling high. And there are times when you want the lower values anyway.

After a few plays, though, I do wonder if being the first player provides too significant an advantage, since that player (and the second player) will have two opportunities to go first, while players 3 and 4 do not. Also, I'm convinced that an economic strategy will almost certainly defeat someone who goes after the black cards.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This odd game gets my vote for Most Creative Use of Dice, bumping Yspahan from the top spot. By expending the correct resources, you may alter your dice and buy an opponent's dice to enable you to take the most favorable available actions. It's like putting together card combos, but much more fun.

Speaking of cards, there's no shortage of those in this game. They provide victory points, resources, improved dice rolls, and, significantly, replayability. Troyes is an exception to my usual distaste for games that produce variety largely from whatever cards happen to be drawn and re-learned each game.

A lotta slow.  Should try again tho.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Whoever (among reasonably competent players) rolls the highest numbers is going to win. 

Yeah, I know it's rated 7.8 and has mechanisms built in for luck mitigation so that can't be true, but it really is  true.  

Dice are manipulated by spending a secondary currency, 'renown', which is gained by - you guessed it - rolling high numbers. 

Fans will say that it 'balances out' because you roll 'a lot' of dice. You probably roll on average around 20 dice per game. It's very likely that on that few dice some players will roll well above or below the mean.

Troyes has more bells and whistles than Stone Age, but at its heart it has the same central mechanism: [i]roll high and win.[/i]

RATING: 7
Starting this out at an 8 after 1 play.   It's a fun game where you select dice (number and color) and then perform a various action based on the dice selected.  The dice mechanic is the part that makes it unique.  Looking past the dice mechanic, it is the common mechanic of "Take this, convert to this"..."Now take that, convert to this"  Basically converting dice to money, influence, more dice, and ultimately victory points.  There are cards which keep the conversions possibilities unique for each game.  So it seems pretty typical....but yet it's well done. I look forward to another play.

Thinking about bumping this down a point.  I'm not sure it has good replayability.  There are some obvious combos...some of which may be required to have a chance to win.  Also, I didn't like it as much with 4 players.  Seems like a better 3 player game as there is more room on the building cards...and you're less likely to get locked out of them.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Players are family members, guiding the medieval city of Troyes through the construction of a cathedral, while dealing with various misfortunes afflicting the city.  Interesting game, where dice take a central role.  Players place pawns into three major buildings in Troyes, and pawns give their owners the ability to roll dice in that buildings colour.  The die results are plonked on the market and available for use by the owner, or for purchase by the other players.  Dice totals are used to combat the woes befalling Troyes, construct the cathedral, use various artists and tradesmen in Troyes, and so on.  The purpose is to keep an engine running, making sure the money, influence and victory points are ticking over nicely.  Lots of nice stuff here, quite a simple game in terms of rules, but the decision tree is almost overwhelming at first - with a lot of cogs and mechanisms spinning inside the game.  Be prepared for a lot of staring and saying "uhhh" in the first game, but it improves rapidly after that.  Having 27 craftsman in a game where only 9 appear ensures it should stay fresh, and kudos for at least feeling a little different from most of the stuff coming out these days.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
My top game of 2010. The way dice are used is well done - the game turns the idea of "good rolls" and "bad rolls" completely on its head. Low rolls can be just as good as high rolls, and the game also gives players ways to change the result of rolls when a different result is needed (re-rolling and flipping). Players can also buy dice from other players for a cost that increases proportionally to the benefit that will be received. The game also uses cards to create a complex exchange rate system among the four other main game elements: pips, money, influence points, and victory points.

RATING: 7.75
This game is vaguely reminiscent of one of my favourite games: [thing=27162][/thing].

The presentation seems rather lackluster.  The artwork reminded me of [thing=444][/thing], and was surprised that [boardgameartist=12282][/boardgameartist] was not part of the [thing=444][/thing] team.

Game play must be experienced.  Tons of dice rolling (which is the number one appeal to me), but the decisions to be made!  Players prone to AP will certainly experience those moments, but overall, downtime isn't bad - as long as you don't plan too far ahead!  It is almost as if you shouldn't decide what to do until it is your turn since the dice-pool created will look nothing like it did on your previous turn.

If you are looking for a deeper implementation of a familiar premise, this is the one for your collection.  If you are looking for something more approachable with much more appealing artwork, stick with [thing=27162][/thing].

RATING: 8
Good and very thinky dice rolling and dice manipulation game.  Not to mention dice stealing, dice re-rolling, dice flipping, and dice destruction.   There are two currencies (coins, influence) to manage.  Event cards are scored similar to the buildings in Blue Moon City.   Players have secret objectives but all players share in the scoring, so there is a little bit of deduction happening as well.  There is pseudo-worker placement and engine building.   Not so much engine building on a grand scale, but rather lots of action spaces that are like mini-machines.   There is a ton of choice in the game, which is going to give it a lot of appeal, I would think.   On the downside, it is utterly themeless and fairly ugly graphically (although it is stylized and well-done in a red/yellow/white/black color scheme, it just doesn't appeal to me, but it is unique).   A dream come true for AP players.

RATING: 9

RATING: 9
The ultimate dice rolling euro.  At the beginning of the game, people will place workers in one of three buildings types: civilian, military, and religion.  For each person in a building you'll roll that many dice of the appropriate type.  These dice basically have 4 functions: to use with the activity cards, combat event cards, build the cathedral, and place more people into 3 building types.  Some of these options allow you to group multiple dice together and usually more pips will give you greater benefits (ala Stone Age).  The big twist with this game compared to other dice euros, is that you can pay resources to other players and use their dice.  The more dice you for a single action, the more expensive the price is for each individual dice you are purchasing.

There are strategic avenues to pursue but you really need to tactically adjust your plans based on what other people are doing and based on what dice are available on your own turn.  More than any other dice euro, high rolls will not determine a winner.  High and low rolls have benefits, though high rolls are typically better. Even though higher rolls are still typically more preferable, there are many ways to mitigate the luck such as: buying opponents high rolls, re-rolling/flipping your own dice using influence, and the unique aspects of some of the activity cards.

I find this game to have a higher replay value than a lot of other euro's in the fact that you only use 9 of the 27  activity cards each game (30 if you have the promo cards).  You will also only use a portion of the available secret goal and event cards.  That means each game, there will new combos to try and different incentives to maneuver in various ways. Troyes is better than Castles of Burgundy, Airships, Yspahan, and Kingsburg.  It's probably better than Alien Frontiers too but I have yet to play it.  You owe it to yourself to try this title.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.6
Every time I play this game I like it more because of the layers and depth of strategy it offers. Pros: Quick to set up; relatively simply to understand once you get used to the icons; replayability; depth and variety of strategy; the event cards offer a semi-cooperative aspect to the game where everyone is incentivized to overcome them as quickly as possible; when someone takes one or more of your dice it's not that bad because (1) they have to pay you for each die that they take, and (2) it adds to the strategy and tension in the game because you can't count on having all of your dice at the start of your next turn and when someone takes a die you were going to use you have to react and formulate a new strategy; the game scales very well, although it plays differently (which I like because it adds variety). So many positives! Cons: the rule book is TERRIBLE (a friend of mine and I tried to learn from the rules at the Dice Tower Con and it seemed so complicated to the point we almost gave up, then someone offered to walk us through the first round and it all made sense); on that note, it's not the easiest game to teach (maybe just having people watch you play a round would be the best way instead of trying to explain the game); someone might not like the "take that" feeling when someone grabs one of the dice in your district; an AP-prone person could get stuck thinking through the combinations of dice actions on any given turn.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
My first play of Troyes seems to suggest that the game does live up to its hype. It is a worker placement game, but its take on the genre is that it is more tactical than most WP games that I like. You are making long term strategical decisions, yes. But how well those pan out is determined by the luck of the roll and the allocation of your hard earned resources. On the whole, I'm pretty fed up with worker placement games; I find that the best WP games are just explicitly better than their counterparts. That being said, I may have room in my collection for another. 

Another play shows me that the game's tactical nature makes it shine. It's five turns long, and there is a lot of unknown information. That being said, you do not have much time to figure out what you want to do, and commit to it. What colors of dice you are getting will help you figure out what your best ideas will be, but you'll have to wait to see the events, the actions, and what your opponents do before you get an opportunity to act on what your best ideas may be. 

This game seems particularly unforgiving for first time players. Money can be a hard resource to acquire, I would suggest to beginners to look for investments for future turns, be it more dice, prestige, or money. 

My only complaint against this game is that I think its real sweet spot is at exactly three players. With four there is a lot of competition for the dice and the efficiencies that can be obtained are low. So low that the events can really plow you over. The game I have played with four was my least favorite play of this game so far. Conversely I am not interested in trying this title with two, as I think it would be boring. Troyes is a game about shortages, and with only two players (and the glut of dice that would be had with only two) the play would, IMO, detract from the true experience of Troyes. I'm pretty obsessive about optimal player numbers in games in general, though. 

8->9. I no longer consider three players essential, it may not even be the optimal number for me anymore. The tactics, lucky-but-not-too-lucky dice rolls, randomization of cards, and the seemingly mupltiple routes to victory really my plays of this title enjoyable.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
The best dice strategy game out there, hands down IMO. The game mixes worker placement with bonus power combinations and, well, a lot of other stuff into a strategic treat. The Achilles Heel of dice games, higher rolls, has finally been solved. High rolls are not necessarily better than low ones since people will be tempted to purchase your higher dice out from under you, and you can flip your low dice (e.g., 1 > 6). Three colors of dice are used in three different neighborhoods, each with a different contribution to the proceedings, and you manage two currencies, influence and money. You can pull off some slick moves in the game and I'm constantly engaged with what options I may or may not have when it comes to my turn. The mechanism of buying each other's dice makes for great tension in what move you should take now vs dice you'll leave exposed.

There's a steep learning curve with the iconography and the cards, so teaching is not the easiest. There's great variability in play as you only use a combination of 9 action cards each game (with the expansion cards which I recommend, that's 9 of 30 cards). While Castles of Burgundy also addresses the high-low dice issue, I find this one far more strategic, interactive, and overall engaging from turn to turn.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Although thematically a single-sentence description of [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/18602/caylus]Caylus[/url] would almost fit here, Troyes is mechanically unique and original. It could be described as a two-step worker placement game: you place workers to get dice which you then use to activate actions (reminiscent of [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/27162/kingsburg]Kingsburg[/url]). Alternatively, we could define it as a collection of simultaneous area-majority mini-games, as a gamer friend of mine once did. There are two dozen multicolored dice in the box, but chance does not dominate the game; their real role is to expand the decision tree. As in many other eurogames, you have to feed (in this case, "pay") your workers, but money flows around fast enough due to the constant buying of each other's dice. Troyes has a nice sense of narrative coming from the progressively interesting and powerful actions that are discovered in turns 1 to 3. It is a dynamic puzzle which grows before our eyes. I'm a fan of Alexandre Roche's art in the board and cards: it helps in sustaining a veneer of theme on what is essentially a complex abstract game. I must admit this game is not the most elegant and intuitive design out there: every time I play it, I have to consult the rule book a couple of times during the game to check one of its many small-print details. Nevertheless, Troyes is still one of my favorite eurogames ever and my enjoyment only grows each time I play.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Traded for Mage Knight.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I just recently played Alien Frontiers (another dice game that allocates the dice according to the pips).  Where Alien Frontiers is a light to medium weight game that tends to drag on, this one is a strong medium weight game with more strategy and not enough time!  This is  more of a gamer's game of area control and resource management, with military engagements, co-operation, deduction, and bluffing.

Numerous options are available every turn.  Or so it seems at the beginning--but options start dwindling and it is a race to get what you want.  

Your best dice will likely be bought from underneath you, so a little hard to plan your turn until it rolls around.  However the game allows for a clever change of numbers on your dice by turning the dice upside down (with a cost, of course).  So even though it is a dice game, this ability to change the numbers so drastically removes it from strongly luck-based dice games which I don't like.

This is another game where learning the cards and especially card combinations is important, which means frequent plays is better than pulling it out once in a long while.  Luckily it is short enough (sometimes an hour), so that a couple of games can be played in an evening.

There is a lot going on in this game.  Players must work together to keep potentially run-away winners at bay--not a good game for those who like to plan a course of action and stick to it.  This is also a subtle game of deduction!  Everyone gets a secret victory point goal, a goal that all can benefit from.  If players can determine other's goals and take advantage, then end of game VP tallying can have a surprise ending.  Therefore, a bit of bluffing can also enter the game.  This is also a math game with conversions between money/influence/VP, and can lead to a bit of pondering.  

The only reason I'd play a two-player game again is to learn the cards better.  

A simple, attractive game with nice components.  Extremely well balanced. Many facets of play and a changing setup, makes replay-ability high.  For me a strike against it, is that if I am an infrequent player, it's hard to beat a player who knows the cards and card combinations.  And if players don't cooperate, more than likely there will be a runaway winner--so your crowd has to be right, otherwise the same person might win every time.

Edit:  Rating raised.

RATING: 9.75
"DICE THAT AREN'T DICE"

-Some buddy: 24 dice??? has a lot of luck???
-BGG  buddy: not an all!!! it's Troyes!!!

A game that uses 24 dice but where luck factor isn't determinant at all, must be called "Dice wich aren't dice". This fact, indeed, is enough for me to take my hat out off my head and say two things:
-Firstly (to the creators): "Congratulations. What an original piece of board-gaming-art!"
-Secondly (to Parker Brother's spawns and shit like that): "Look what kind of use you could give to dice... Learn something of Troyes"

I'm in love with this game! Imho: The better dice game ever! In fact, they should have called this game: "Dice that aren't dice". All it's mechanics are so well designed and combines in such perfect ways!

One of the thing I like the most about Troyes is that it has too many ways to get VP (victory points) but you always have to fight against your oponents in order to get them... Yeah, in Troyes there's a lot of interaction-confrontation between players and, speaking by myself, I really love it!.

Another strong aspect: In Troyes you can earn not just the yet classical VP (victory points), but IP (influence points) also, and the ways you can spend them during the game has been so well thought, that even now, after have been played arround 25 games, still surprises me, and it's because it can be possible to changing an apparently bad situation in just seconds, of course just if you planify well, but not just what to do, but when to use that valuable IP.  

In addition: It's components are nice and of good quality and I love that kind of medieval artwork-style that fits in such a perfect way with the theme of the game.

In resume: a must have for lovers of "german-style" board games.

¡WANT TO PLAY MORE AND MORE!

RATING: 7.5
A Troyes kivitelezése nagyon szép, és elég egyedi a grafikája. Ez valakinek nem annyira tetszik, ugyanis egy csöppet sötét, komor a hangulata. A játék során munkásokat rakunk le, az egyre több akciólehetőség valamelyikére és/vagy használjuk őket. A játékban a várost külső veszély fenyegeti, ami minden körben egyre több szívást generál a játékosoknak. A veszéllyel közösen többé-kevésbé megküzdve, kell minél több pontot szerezni az öt körös játék végére. 
- A játék nagyon szép, de valóban kicsit komor
- A játékszabályok meglehetősen bonyodalmasak, nem gémereknek szerintem esélytelen megtanítani őket
- A játék nagy előnye pl. az alien frontiers-hez, képest, hogy van valamennyi lehetpőséged a másik köre alatt is előre gondolkodni, így lénygesen kevesebb a holt idő.
- A véletlenszerű akciókártyák meglehetősen nagy újrajátszási faktort adnak a játékhoz

Sajnos nem sikerült még az agyamnak megfelelő dice placement-tet találni. A túl sok lehetősget szinte képtelenség áltátni, és ez rengeteg gondolkodást, és a játék végére teljes brain burn-t eredményez. A játék után hazajöttem, és bedőltem az ágyba. :)
Érdekelnek az általam csak dice placement-nek nevezett játékok, de valahogy eddig egyik se tudod felhőtlen szórakozást generálni. Ettől függetlenül a Troyes egy jó játéknak tartom, amit ha megvenni nem is fogok, befogható maradok egy-egy játékra.
