=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
The unusual use of dice is what sets Troyes apart from other resource conversion games. The dice provide resources but can also be purchased from other players who may then end up without any resources themselves. This novel mechanism is bolted onto a fairly normal foundation of worker placement and resource conversion.

Troyes is a dice game, and what players roll does matter, but there are three points that reduce the impact of dice results. Firstly, the ability to (forcibly) buy them off other players means you can get supplies you need from elsewhere, secondly, there are quite a few buildings you can use to modify die results when you have workers present, and thirdly, there are usually options available even for low rolls.

The game is a bit schizophrenic in that it goes to great lengths to lessen the impact of the dice which ultimately sort of turns it into "yet another resource conversion game" (TM). The implementation of a neat game mechanism is somewhat more noteworthy than the game as a whole. That is not to say it's a bad game, though.

March 1, 2011 - 3 plays later, I really like it. One of the better games from Essen 2011, but not sure how often this will get played. After three quick plays, it got shuffled to the back in favor of London, Navegador, and 7 Wonders. However, I hope to get it back into play once those games get over their initial shine.

Have now played it 4 times and am playing it for a fifth time at work (during lunch breaks). I have to say it has now surpassed London and Navegador for me. I still love 7 W, but it doesn't have the depth of Troyes. 7W is what it is - my favorite "quickie." But when i want a main dish - Troyes is currently at the top of the menu!

RATING: 6
Troyes was a bit of a letdown. It feels like a game that is disconnected from itself in many different places. Honestly, none of the parts of the game made any coherent sense to me at that point. It turns out it is a rather easy game to understand, but by the end I felt that the parts of the game just didn't come together to make any sort of fluid game. I like that it has replayability in terms of the action cards, but it is such a mash of dozens of ways to make points and I don't think one really knows how to play this game well until they've played it quite a few times and see the interplay between actions that really have no solid connection on the outset. Troyes, in that sense is a weird game for me: it has all the elements of games I like, but just fell flat. In the end, I just didn't have any fun. I felt I had spent two hours trying to crunch the mechanics. That experience isn't something I normally shy away from, but I felt Troyes lacked tension almost the entire time, which made it a somewhat boring experience. I didn't feel particularly rewarded when I made a rather complex move as it just seemed like the best thing at the time given what I was given. To sum it up, Troyes is a solid game, very well-thought out, obviously designed by experts in the field, and a total boring experience. I would play again, but perhaps with three players (instead of four) to see if the increase of control and quickness of turns would keep the life going in this one.

RATING: 4.5
Somewhat chaotic dice-based scoring mechanics, which reminds me of Masons.  You're looking for the best use of a color of dice, from a pool of all players dice, potentially stealing away (via purchase) other players dice during the round.  I found the game entirely forgettable, although I know people who like these types of problems.  Some actions are stored up for later use, and so there are definitely some creative ways to try and make more potent scoring moves.  There's enough flexibility that the rolls themselves are rarely frustrating, which is nice for a die-rolling game. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
Way too much time needed to invest in a repetitive game where you can get unduly influenced by random events and other players secret victory conditions.  Have to become familiar with many cards and complex VP/$/influence conversions. Have to analyze too many different event/activity conversions to play fast or fun.  Kingsburg, while not the world's greatest game, is much faster and funner, and the learning curve not nearly so steep. 

Once again, I don't understand the hype. If you're a "gamer" who has played every Euro and needs a new fix I suppose, have at it.  If you're looking for fun or a game that is not a math exercise, look elsewhere.

RATING: 8
Fantastic game.  Unique dice mechanics.  Deep strategy.  Lots of variety and replayability.  Works well with 2P and up.  Very close games.

UPDATE: My wife and I aren't in love with the theme in this one.  It's a little dry, and the medieval artwork doesn't seem to help us identify with our character cards or care much about what's going on in the city, or feel much threat from the black dice.  Mechanically, the game is incredible.  Strategically, it's deep.  Thematically?  I guess it's just not as much fun in this regard as it could have been.  But we enjoy the game and so far have decided to keep and pull it out on occasion.  I'm interested to see if more players will improve our feeling of it any.

UPDATE:  It's been a few months since our last play. We played last night and had the same "meh" feeling throughout the game.  There was plenty of tension, hard decision-making, moments of press your luck, plenty of thinky puzzles, and solid competition, but we just didn't feel like we cared about the city all that much, more like we were just going through the motions of the game, maybe more like a math puzzle.  We played Yedo the previous evening and in some ways the games are similar: they each have cards with symbols on them :P, but in Yedo, each card has a bit of story on it, and somehow this really pulls us into the game and encourages us to care about our clan and reinforce the ideas that we're competing against other clans and reminds us that we want to be the best clan in the city, so that's why we're making all of these moves.

I'm not 100% sure if we're going to trade this one away yet or not.  I appreciate the game for a long list of reasons, but I'm frustrated that I"m not in love with it and I really want to know why.  :P  

UPDATE: we decided to sell the game.  While we appreciate it for its unique use of dice as worker placement, we decided that it doesn't have enough theme for us, and the mechanics are too mathematical and the iconography too hard to remember and interpret, which further removes us from the theme.  Sad to see this one go, but hopefully the next person will enjoy it more.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Troyes is a weird game. At its core, it's what I call an "Ystari game" for lack of a better term. I'm referring to the type of game in which the players take turns doing small actions: earning money or influence or victory points or goods, or converting one of these into another, or putting a dude on the board that will generate more of these things later on. Caylus is the obvious example, but many of the games published by Ystari follow this general pattern. They are games that give players a broad menu of actions, and challenge them to use those actions in the most efficient way possible.

I typically don't like that style of game, so my labeling something "an Ystari game" is usually a criticism. But Troyes features one marvelous innovation that makes it truly interesting. In Troyes, the menu of actions is contained within each player's handful of dice. And we're not just talking about dice with action icons like War of the Ring. In Troyes, each of the three colors of dice can be used for several different actions. The effects of these actions will depend upon the number of dice you're able to use, the numbers showing on those dice, and the particular cards available on the board.

While playing Troyes, I've found that almost every turn presents tricky decisions. It's difficult to know what to do first, because other players can purchase your dice right out from under you, and of course you can do the same to them. There are a couple different ways to manipulate the numbers on the dice, but you're only allowed to modify your own dice. This means that rolling "badly" is sometimes better than rolling "well." Your good rolls will quickly be scooped up by your opponents, while your bad rolls can be changed into good rolls once you're ready to use them.

Troyes is remarkably polished and developed for a game from a new publisher and three young designers (Sébastien Dujardin, Xavier Georges, and Alain Orban). But there are a couple minor points that some gamers might call flaws. First, going early in the turn order is a strong advantage, and despite a rotating start player, not everyone gets an equal crack at it. Secondly, there is occasional strangeness surrounding the secret patrons that will award points for various achievements at the end of the game. You can try to predict which patrons will score based on your opponents' actions (each players knows about one of them, but everyone scores for each patron that's in the game). But if one of your opponents is doing a poor job of catering to their patron's whims, then you'll probably guess wrong, and the point awards may feel arbitrary or random.

RATING: 8
Troyes is a very good strategy game that uses dice in a clever way.
The goal of the game is to have the most victory point.  It can be overwhelming at first because there's so many things that you can do during your turn.

I love the use of the dice in this game!  Not too random, you can use influence point to re-roll dice.  You can also buy dice from other player.
I also like that the activity cards are different each game so you have to adjust your strategy depending on what is available.

RATING: 10
Maybe my favourite game. I love having a proponent of chance in strategy games. The dice and the random selection of activity/event cards keep every game feeling fresh and exciting, yet the game is nuanced enough for proper strategies to be formed.

If you like being slapped upside the head by drought and heresy, or if you like games that allow players to be creative with their moves, you should check out Troyes!

The only con I can think of is that Troyes might take a couple plays to really feel at home with the mechanisms. For some people, the first game can leave a confusing taste, so teach it well and offer lots of tips!

It´s not really possible to follow a certain strategy as a bad die roll (or even a great one) would not work (either too few pips or so many that other player
will buy your dice, leaving you with money but no dice.) My first play was already with the purple die from the 
expansion which is on one hand a joker and on the other it cannot be bought by your fellow players (and somehow saved the game from a bad rating).
The problem in general is: it was not that fun. The game is bone dry and the theme is barely present. You count and try to optimize your turn with pushing around some cubes. Playing early in a turn is important, going last sucks. I am an Eurogamer but this big dice game felt really dryer as others like Kingsburg or Stone Age. Will I play again? Yes, but not that soon.

RATING: 8
There's a good game in here, but I have taught this to three groups of people who have all since refused in practice to play it again. It's not a fun game to teach repeatedly, and it is especially unforgiving to inexpert play. Basically, the symbology in this game is like a brick wall. Will continue to attempt to get this to the table, but I may soon give up.

EDIT: Sold off, but rebought at MSRP after it was OOP. Tentatively, we tried it again....and loved it! The criticisms above still stand; symbology is obscure, and there is a distinct advantage to knowing some of the synergies beforehand. But for all that, it's a game that snowballs in interest the more you play it, and a great example of the "mitigated luck" genre of Eurogame. Great fun.

RATING: 3
A dice allocation game where rolling high numbers is strictly better than rolling low. That is never a good thing, especially in a game where you roll your dice only six times. You can get dice from your opponents or flip yours, but these options cost valuable resources. Besides, it may be annoying when you are the player whose dice are taken.

The cards are wildly unbalanced and require dice of a determined color, but you must choose your initial dice with no information on which cards will be available. Of course, you may get more dice during the game, but you may also lose them as the other players take theirs, and there is nothing you can do to prevent that.

All in all, I found the amount of randomness just unacceptable for a such an overly long, complex and convoluted game. And the bad iconography surely does not help. Also, the ugly art and generic medieval theme fail to disguise the complete abstractness. For dice placement, I will take "Kingsburg" or "Alien Frontiers" over this any time.

RATING: 6
It moves at a nice clip, and the dice system is indeed rather innovative (it's not as similar to Kingsburg as people would have you believe). The cards do provide an awful lot of variability.
The biggest strike against Troyes for me is that the game feels too much like point-mongering--there are 27 ways to score points, and your job is to eke slightly more points out of each transaction. That's not bad per se, but I'm a little burned out on such exercises--which I guess goes to show how much of a jaded Eurogamer I am, because Troyes really does have a fair number of strengths.

RATING: 6
I enjoyed this but the problems are too much for it to overcome. Hugely turn order dependent especially with three players.  Maybe that would be fixed with 4?  The iconography would be better replaced with text.  The luck factor with the dice is super low, it really doesn't contribute much randomness to the game.  Your choices each turn are restricted a little by the results of your rolls, but depending on the board situation it won't effect anything.  Overall with the dice flipping/ reroll mechanic you can fix any particularly unlucky turns.  So why are we using dice in the first place, if the only thing they do is very rarely screw someone?   Finally, the math is obnoxious.  Yes its only counting pips and dividing by 3, 4, or something, but when you are evaluating a dozen or so possible moves thats a lot of silly math, especially when you consider the possibilities of stealing dice.   I would like to try it again to make sure but i do not think it is for me.

What did I like about it?  Although the rulebook isn't presented in the most understandable way, once you got going it was very intuitive for its level of complexity.  It also had a lot of different ways to win and different strategies, which was my favorite thing about the game.  I did not invest in military dice at all during our game, and instead concentrated on a strategy that allowed me to steal other player's dice for cheap.  Then I snagged a couple spots that let me turn those dice into money and victory points and I had a little engine going.  The other players were concentrating on defeating the event cards and using military dice to do different things.  I believe the fact that I was left alone to my own devices contributed to my win, otherwise I think the strategies were pretty equal.  Definitely the strong point of this game, the multiple different strategies.  It has some fantastic replayability in my opinion.

The too long, didn't read version is that I didn't like it, but it had some good ideas.  I want to play it again in case I am selling it short in some way.  Obviously it is very highly rated, so maybe I am?

RATING: 2
Wasn't convinced after the first play; second play confirmed this was a stinker.  2 points for the nice art and three language rulebook (French, German, English).

Game is way too long for what it is, an advanced worker placement game, in which you can become a spectator after the 1st turn (out of 6).

Re-read the rules (in both French and English) after first game to ensure we were playing this correctly.  Perhaps we just had unlucky event draws??  With no influence to modify/re-roll poor dice rolls, and not enough cash to make a decent die combo, was relegated to the status of spectator, trying to wrestle workers from other players who had Influence, Workers and cash to spare....  Uphill battle to attempt to stay on the positive side of the VP score...

Started off with 4 "Workers" in town, lost 3 (max you can lose in a turn) due to other player placements/actions, and managed to recoup one.

Started 2nd turn with 2 workers (dice), Influence wiped out by event, 7$, and attacking units tied up 1 out of 2 dice.  With my last die, was able to place one worker, and I lost 1 during the turn, thus down to two at start of 3rd turn.

Influence event still in force, wiped out my Influence, again.  "Invading" units wiped out my dice.  With one point of influence, little money, and no dice to generate income, I had to pass after trying to get another worker in town...  Half the game gone, 2 VP showing....  

Finished the game with 3 VP, about 25 points behind 3rd place.  Was never able to recoup the loss of 3 dice in the 1st turn.

Game winner, in both games, was the player with the most dice, from turn to turn.  Out of 4 players, 2 are in the race to win, the other 2 are unable to catch up and are mere spectators.

Will not be playing this one again.  For trade.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
In an ever growing sea of Euro economic optimization (which are sometimes economic snowball games), it's refreshing to see (and play) a game which still looks, feels, and plays like a economic/resource conversion Euro without either the (single-player-solitaire) sandbox or the snowball (i.e., make money engine, convert to points mid-to-late game) formula. Rather, this is an interactive (through buying other player's dice, thus messing with potential plans) and non-snowballing (resource) economies (i.e., while the values of particular resources change in response to which patrons appear, it modifies the valuation in a linear rather than exponential manner). As the patrons that appear each game are different, there is no preset narrative to play within (I'm looking at you Agricola and Le Havre) but rather this games requires an ability to respond tactically to the way in which the game flows. Also, while I realize that the art might be love/hate, I find it very fresh, distinct, and beautiful.

RATING: 8
I got quite into this and was easily considering voting 7.5 by the half way point. There were tough tactical decisions in every round.

Unfortunately I didn't realise at the start of the game the necessity of getting 2 high valued red spaces or the necessity to get and generate influence reliably. This said I think that even if I play it better next time there are inherent problems with the game.

Money is far too easy to come by, you can be making 10+ a turn quite easily and unless you have enough influence there is nothing that can be done with it, furthermore there is no way of turning money into influence.

So you have a larger than necessary (even to get you 6 VPs on the money bonus card at the end of the game) going into the last turn. Well as first player that's fine, who cares what dice you had when you can pay 18 and steal three off other people?

This makes going first in the final round a little over powered and tarnishes the overall quality of the game

upon second playing I got even more into this, and then realised that my innitial assesment is even more true than I had thought for that final round or so

Money is just not scarce enough

----

It would seem that the first couple of times of playing we just had a strange combination of cards come out which made money so abundent. It's actually a pretty decent game. Perhaps if there were some way in the set up phase of making it so that they come out with a better distribution then it would be better still.

It was better with 2 than it was with four though I can see that the BGG consensus of best with 3 is probably correct.

Lots more analysis and blocking could be done with the lower number of players, it's the same reason I love Stone age with 2

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
PRO:
- the mix of worker placement, action cards and dice as resources may sound like any other eurogame, but the game concept feels incredibly fresh and different
- medieval theme fits wonderfully, actions make sense in what they are supposed to represent
- the game just "flows" very naturally, once you have grasped the rules
- lots of player interaction (buying and selling dice, taking positions in buildings)
- simple game winning condition: most victory points win
- medieval look, beautiful art concept
- high re-playability factor thanks to different event, action and bonus cards
- dice as resources, but luck factor is minimized
- plays surprisingly fast (around 90 minutes with 4 players)
- almost no downtime, you don't have to wait long for the next turn
- (partly) hidden victory points add to the tension

CONTRA:
- not the game you want to teach to non-gamers, due to "different from usual"-kind of rules

VERDICT:
I absolutely love this game. You always have an option to pursue, because there's always a way to win victory points, even though your opponent just did THAT move that you wanted to take yourself. It feels so fresh and new, there's almost no downtime... an instant classic!

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
1.起始每人一張角色卡(兩人遊戲兩張)!!!角色卡任務每人都可以計分!!!!
   影響力4點，5元
=>放市民由起始順時針放再逆時針回來
!!!事件卡4人6張，3人5張，2人4張!!!
最終計分: a,行動卡上市民分數 b.角色卡每人都算 c.未被解決事件卡1張1分 d.大教堂扣分。
2. 遊戲流程
A.翻開行動卡
B.領10元，付薪水
C.執骰(包含中立玩家)
D.解決事件卡(從起始開始，每個玩家選數字最高對抗，紅骰點數兩倍，解決1顆=1點影響力。)
E.行動選擇
a.執行行動卡(必須要先派人到分數格，有人可以直接執行)，放人就必須要執行卡片行動。
b.建設教堂，每排最後沒有建設的人要扣分，放置必須從低階開始
c.對抗事件(最多方塊得到較大分數，平手平分總和向下取整，放置最多方塊拿取事件卡，平手則先放方塊者先拿。)
d.放置市民到建築物，可以擠掉別人市民，被擠掉的人會平躺在建築物，若該建築物有該色市民可免於被擠，市民若移走去執行其他行動就失效。
e.農業 黃骰領2元
f.Pass領2元，每次輪到你就領1元。若所有玩家Pass或地區沒有骰子回合結束。

擴充:
每回合可以使用一顆骰子移動城牆守衛，1點1步，可以用錢加點數，最多6點，然後可以啟動行動格，每回合每個行動格最多啟動1次。
1. 我能不能放商人到行動卡上而不啟動該卡能力？
(例： 拿點數2的骰子 去啟動"點數/3"的卡片，只為了放商人)

Ans: 不行。 必須至少啟動1次。
另外，如果該行動卡是轉換東西(影響力->錢)，必須至少轉換1次，更多轉換則是選擇性。
(例: 1影響力->6元，若你點數足夠啟動4次，可選擇只轉換2次。)
如果該行動卡不是轉換東西，則必須將完整啟動次數用盡。
目的是不讓玩家只單純為了分數而放商人。

2. 放到3棟主要建築物的人從哪裡來？ 商人的人從哪裡來？

Ans: 兩個問題的答案是一樣的，你可以從個人供應區拿人(可能必須花2點影響力買人)，
或是從場上任一個位置拿人(已存在的商人、在主要建築物的人、被推出倒在一旁的人)。
若你是從場上拿人，則該位置會維持空著，直到有人執行行動將它佔滿。

3. 能不能從一張卡拿回商人，再花錢擺回同一張卡當商人？

Ans: 可以。有可能原本的商人是沒有分數的(非前兩位進去的玩家)，之後有一個玩家
將1個商人拉走去其他位置，此時格子空出來了，你可以先拿回這個沒有分數的商人，
再花錢踩進有分數的那個格子，當然同問題1，你必須至少啟動1次卡片。

4. 從主要建築物被推出來倒在一旁，可以保護不再被推，此效用持續到該回合結束嗎？

Ans: 不是。只有當你被推出的人，確實倒在那個建築物裡的時候，你得到保護。
當你將該位被推出的人拿去其他地方工作時，就不再獲得保護，該回合還是可能被推推。
規則上來說，你甚至可以拿起被推出的人，再把同一建築物裡自己的人給推出去。

5. 當我要使用行動卡上面的小方塊時(延時效應的方塊)，行動卡上必須有商人嗎？

Ans: 不用。當要啟動該卡時必須有商人，才能產生小方塊，一旦這些方塊在卡片上，
之後即使移走了該商人，還是可以使用那些小方塊。

6. 非軍事事件(黃卡及白卡)多久會發生？
Ans: 在被解決掉(該事件卡被拿走)之前，每一回合的事件階段都會發生。

7. 事件無法成功解決時的扣2分，什麼情況會發生？
   (即為原po的問題)

Ans: 事件分為3大類： (1)黑骰 (2)個別影響每位玩家 (-2影響力/-3元)
     (3)改變板子上的狀態 (中立玩家移入主要建築物/中立玩家蓋大教堂)
只有第(2)類的事件，會產生扣2分的懲罰。
例: 有一位玩家只剩1點影響力，此時事件階段每位玩家要扣2影響力，則該玩家先扣1點
影響力，然後再扣2分。第(3)類的事件絕不會發生扣2分的狀況，即使事件所需的灰色方塊
數量不足，也不會有人被扣分。第(1)類黑骰，就是在每位玩家輪流抵抗黑骰時，當有玩家
無法成功抵禦時，該玩家會被扣分，這是在所有事件效果已被解決了之後。

8.可以浪費白骰子去建造教堂,但是如果教堂都已經占滿了,則不可以使用白骰.

上面的1(至少必須啟動1次)跟6(事件每回合發生)，是最常見被忽略的地方
最後，自己再補1個，關於pass拿錢的
輪到你時，場上有任何骰子 (不論自己特區還有沒有骰子)
你可以
(1) 用骰子作行動  或著
(2) pass。這時pass會在你的特區放2元，這回合之後每次輪到你，再補1元上去。
回合會在 (a)場上完全沒骰子 或 (b)所有玩家pass 時結束
如果是以(b)的條件結束回合，每個玩家的特區上都會有錢才是正確的情況。

RATING: 9.4
En teoría las mecánicas "dados sin suerte" + "objetivos ocultos" + "colocación de trabajadores" + "gestión de recursos" funciona bien. En la práctica es un juego muy "marciano". Con tres tipos de recursos (dinero, influencia y PV's) que se interrelacionan de manera intrincada y con valores de equivalencia distintos en cada partida en función de las cartas de acciones, la cosa se complica innecesariamente. Hay demasiadas "mates" dónde (literalmente), cada movimiento que haces tiene un coste distinto, expresado en unidades distintas que dificulta comprararlos. PROS: mecánica innovadora lo de "dados sin suerte", gran rejugabilidad. CONS: 4º jugador en desventaja, muy pesado para ser sólo de dados (letanía eterna de sumas multiplicaciones y divisiones), sin sensación de crecimiento. Sin trama.

[ENG]

The game mechanisms of "dice without luck" + "hidden goals" + "workers placement" + "resource management" work quite good in theory. But in practical sense is a weird game. It has three kinds of resources (money, influence and VP´s) and all of them are interrelated in a complex way with different equivalences between them depending on the cards. It´s unnecessary knotty. There are a lot of maths. Every movement you do has a different cost expressed in different measures, so it´s difficult to compare two movements. PROs: innovative game mechanism (dice without luck), replayability. CONs: 4th player is on disadvantage. Lot of maths (additions, multiplications and divitions) this makes him heavier than it should. There´s no plot an no "developing feeling".

RATING: 7.5
The straight-up textbook Euro counterpart to Alien Frontier's more Ameritrash leanings, I enjoy both but for different reasons.  Troyes packs in a lot of thinkiness into a reasonably short playtime, and I'm a big fan of buying other people's workers out from under them.  It does seem like the game is an Agricola-esque race to find the best synergies between the Activity cards (and hopefully your secret scoring card), so my main worry is that there is really less here than meets the eye and given the fairly small amount of Activity cards that there will be certain combinations that are just inherently better.  My other main worry is that one of players in our group was completely lost during the whole game, but still finished within a few points of the next closest player.  Still, I look forward to playing a few more times to see if it holds up.

http://www.boardgamegeek.com/video/9937/troyes/reviews-without-pants-episode-9-troyes
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Troyes is a great game that plays in a short time.  The game board artwork is different and pleasant and adds impact to the Medieval French city theme.  Dice are rolled by all the players at the beginning of each round and they are allocated to select actions or buy cards.  The wildest part of this game is that players have the option of using the dice rolled by other players by paying gold for them.  There are mechanisms to change the value of very undesirable dice results.  The game offers rich strategy game play and lots of meaningful decisions for players to make on their turn.  This is not an easy game to teach or learn because it is a relatively remote departure from most other games.  It's not easy to really understand what's happening in the game the first play or two.  I would say it's a heavy-medium weight game; not recommended for non-gamers.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Interesting points gathering mechanism/worker placement/dice allocation.  Has expansion, but is too expensive for what you get.  Good game, plays well.  Does tend to run a bit long but remains interesting and very close scoring right to the end.  Much better form of dice worker mechanic than Alien Frontiers as Troyes allows you to manipulate the dice, reroll, and spend them in many diverse ways (as well as buying and using other players dice).

RATING: 8.46
[b]2 Player review with expansion:[/b]

Having played the game with the expansion from the first try, I thought I'd review them together. 

The game is flawless in everything I've seen and played. The theme is spot on, the interaction is tense and exciting without making me want to curse, and the fantastic combos that present themselves systematically over every match are very clever, always original, and fun to figure out. 

There is just nothing that takes away from this game except for the very slight influence of luck. But with numerous ways to mitigate rolls thanks to the influence track, as well as the fact you can just buy dice directly from each other, the element of luck only adds to the tension and excitement of the game-play. 

Sometimes the fortification bonuses from the expansion seem a little overpowered depending on what is available, but they are always available to everyone. It just seems to force your hand slightly, to invest early in the fortifications, so that you don't miss out. 

Overall, I can't recommend this game highly enough and I can;t wait to see the countless combinations of activity cards that will present themselves over the countless plays I will enjoy over the coming years. 

[b]Pros:[/b]

- The activity cards are surprisingly thematic and clever in their use of the theme. 
- Rolling dice has never been so fun (for me).
- It's always exciting to see what activity cards will appear each year, how you can combine them, and what pips you will roll to strategize. 

[b]Cons:[/b]

- 2 player feels very tactical, while 3 players makes it a little more random. 
- The expansion fortifications bonuses force you into an early strategy sometimes.

[b]2015 Rating:[/b] 8.95 [b]Rank:[/b] 20 [b]Plays:[/b] 2
[b]2014 Rating:[/b] 9.95 [b]Rank:[/b] - [b]Plays:[/b] 6

RATING: 4

:thumbsup: refreshingly stylized illustrations and graphic design
:thumbsup: ability to purchase dice your opponents control to mitigate variance and interact
:thumbsup: activity cards alone create 19,683 outset variables, and when combined with the event and character cards, every game will feel different but familiar, making the learning curve a gentle climb after the initial hike through the fog of the first game
:thumbsdown: emphasizes tactical play at the expense of strategy, and that inability to form long a term plan slows the game while players evaluate the best course of action each turn.

RATING: 6.25
Played this one several times in a short timespan, and it's already starting to lose some of its luster. Don't get me wrong...it's an innovative system that I still want to play with, and there are some great elements in here. But the gameplay is very tactical...the in-game reveals of the profession cards and events adds a hefty amount of luck to the mix that can't easily be rectified without rebalancing the number of rounds in the game and/or the turn order rules.

The semi-cooperative events are appealing, but I think Sylla's implementation offers much more interesting decisions.

The fact that someone's strongest play is often to burn some dice at zero benefit to them, simply to prevent someone else from using the dice, is thematic and appropriate but ultimately unsatisfying.

RATING: 8
Really fun dice game, I like that the available actions are randomized every game and that they mostly all seem interesting and strong. And the dice luck is pretty minimal since you don't really own any dice until you use them, you can use any dice you just have to pay for them if they aren't the ones you rolled, and the cost isn't too bad unless you want to use a lot of other dice at once. My biggest problem with the game is similar to that of suburbia, the secret goal cards. I don't like secret goal cards in general, but I especially don't like ones that are affected by what everyone does, since there becomes this minigame of don't give away your cards (and here some of which seem easier than others to hide). But otherwise this is an interesting game of finding the right combo of actions that come out in a given game and position yourself to best use those actions in combination. Each action must be bought into before it can be used and is only usable by at most two people so choosing the right ones to back your strategy and getting to them first is important.

UPDATE: I just played it with four and it seemed less good. I still didn't like the secret goal cards much but it would be easy enough to just have them all on display from the beginning. But with four players we each had very few dice (since the same total number are used independent of player count) and the cards got filled up quickly and I was able to win just hammering the level one red card over and over and making sure I kept at least two guys in the red building every turn, which isn't that exciting. When there are fewer players each one has more dice and thus can get more done, so there's more of the chaining of card abilities together to do interesting stuff, rather than just hammering the same one thing over and over. But I still enjoyed the play in spite of this, and I'm not changing my rating; I'll just be hesitant to play with four in the future.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
The game has some interesting mechanics in acquiring powers from the available cards and using dice to perform actions depending on the dice roll (plus buying other player's dice from them to supliment your own if you choose). Unfortunately while the mechanics are interesting, they don't come together in a balanced (enough) manner. 

A key part of the game is choosing which card(s) to place your meeple on in order to be able to exploit that cards ability for the rest of the game, some cards can work together to create powerful combo's, some combo's are (a lot) more powerful than others. Missing out on a combo can be crippling.

The black dice can at times unevenly affect players, some may get away with spending hardly anything, while another player might have to spend 2 or more dice to combat them. 

The end game goal cards while giving you something to aim for, since all players score points from each others hidden goal cards, it comes down to a bit of luck as to whether you guessed what others players goals were.

Otherwise it's a solid enough game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is a worker placement Euro where your workers are different colored dice that your roll each turn and then can place.  While the dice rolling introduces luck, it is reasonably well buffered.  The dice can be placed on special cards for useful effects, on a defense card to get points by helping fight off invaders, and in town to get more dice (I think it was, getting a little fuzzy).  I really wanted to like this one but the theme is very shallow so rather abstract and there didn't seem to be a lot of strategy.  It's mostly a very tactical game where you are maximizing payoff each turn without worrying too much about future moves, as far as I could tell.  I didn't not enjoy myself but it didn't leave me with any special desire to play again.

RATING: 9
Game of 2010 for me! 
Incredible idea. 

I hate dices, really. But I do not feel any luck in Troyes. You can use your influence to change your dices, you can buy them to other players, place some little value lots on the athedral or on the different houses.

It is deep and you always have a lot of possibilities. What I love it that you can always come back in the game, even if your first moves were "bad".

You easily think "I am making a cool combo" and it is a pleasure to feel that.

The only negative point is that it is too short :-p
I would like to play it longer and longer!

RATING: 8
2012-05-02

Initial Rating : 7.5 (April 2012)

Best dice game I have played, light-years better than Kingsburg and Alien Frontiers. Surprisingly Troyes has a very low luck factor due to the dice themselves as you can buy them from your opponents, the event and activity cards add to the randomness but in this case I like it very much.

The playtime is generally short, you have lots of interesting decisions and, as the activity cards change from game to game, it has great replayability.

The dice mechanic is brilliant and it is the first time it really excites me to play with dice in an strategy game :D.

Perhaps its greatest weakness is that it seems the game gives an unfair advantage to the first and second player as they are always assured to begin 2 rounds. I´m not sure about that but I plan to play many more times to review this point.

For now I love the game and I can´t wait to play it again.

RATING: 8
The game that fired Kingsburg.  Troyes sets a new standard for weight in a dice game; I hadn’t thought a medium-heavy dice game was possible but it seems to work very well here.  There’s some luck in what die values are rolled but randomness seems to have a much lower  effect on the game than player skill.
  
The iconography is a bit confusing on a first play; I actually had an easier time learning Race for the Galaxy when it first came out, then I did Troyes.  I am especially bad at deciphering icons however.

I need to play more to decide how much I like this game but it’s definitely a good one and a game I’ll consider buying.

Second play update - I'm concerned about turn order.  Not for balance reasons; I think the military obligations balance out any turn order advantage.  But it's harder to make big moves as a late player in the turn order, and I'm worried that might make the game less fun for players 3 and 4.

RATING: 10
Picked up the expansion and have also played it more, recently. Those combined up my ranking to a 10. With 6 cards per card location (9 total) there are over 10 million possible combinations all but guaranteeing every game you play is unique.

The dice worker implementation is unique, fresh and well designed. The fact that you don't own the dice you roll gives the game tension and a lot of interaction.

Typical of a lot of euro games, you never have enough actions for everything you want to, which is a good thing! Can't get enough of this game.

There's a reprint coming, and so I highly recommend getting this game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
This is a semi cooperative deduction game!
I love love love that everybody gets an end of game scoring condition and although they alone get to see it, everyone will score from it. That's the deduction part, watching how everybody plays to try to figure out what end game scoring they have. 
The game itself was a little hard to learn and the art put my wife off from wanting to play it. But half a game in and you begin to pick the game up. It's well worth getting through that first game though, especially as at the end of the game you will want to play it again right away and think you will know how to play better!
It is a puzzley game (I love that) and although it may appear lucky given the dice role, it's more about optimizing. Very similar to castles of burgundy that way. This is a great game and will be in my collection for a long long time.

It's an imaginatively conceived game, well designed.  It suffers from some degree of chaos.

Players are gradually confronted with a growing list of special powers that they can buy and use.  Each one "does stuff" with one of the three colors of dice.  Some get points, some money, some change the pips on other dice (thereby creating synergies.)

Players earn certain quantities of dice, in those three colors, by placing workers there.  Roll your dice, and in turn, apply them to your powers, or buy new ones.

The good and bad part of this is that a poor roll isn't the end of the world - you can buy other people's dice.  But they can buy yours, sometimes really making it difficult to execute a plan.

I don't win this game, so clearly I don't fully get the right way to play it.  It's a design I respect, even though it often infuriates me.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Troyes was the dark horse of Spiel 2010 gamers' games like Vasco da Gama in the year before. While everybody expected 7 Wonders and London to be hits, this soared above the other releases out of the blue. But as with Vasco, it's not so special for me. It's decent, I like to play it again but it is by no means the next big thing.
Its differentiating feature is the unique use of dice. You roll a lot of dice but it doesn't matter much if rolls are low or not as you can buy high rolls from other players and even for low dice rolls there's often something that can be done with them. In this field Troyes scores against other dice-rollers like Kingsburg, Yspahan or Airships. Troyes is another proof of gamers fondness for complexity. The upfront rules investment is heavy and during the first one or two games players will repeatedly have to look up the meaning of all the little symbols. Troyes has yet to prove if the complexity ends up providing a deep game or just an illusion of possibilities. Compared with other dice-rollers, it loses the lightness that makes these games enjoyable. To sum up my impression, Troyes is a dice game for gamers that don't like to roll dice. While a paradox at first sight, this seems to be a fair description of the BGG crowd. It's also the first game by Xavier Georges I rather enjoyed (at least finally as my first reaction was more of the 'what the heck' type). With Royal Palace and especially Carson City, he created two of my least liked worker placement games before.
While initially deterred by the artwork depicted online, I enjoyed it in actual gameplay where it doesn't look so drab.

RATING: 5
While the theme in Troyes is an absolute bore - bland city influence like every other game - it benefits greatly from nice artwork (finally some euro artwork I can get behind) that feels evocative and seemingly new mechanisms.

While there is nothing really new in the dice - you could find traces of Yspahan, Kingsburg or Stone Age in here, it's hard to explain what makes this feel so much different.  Obviously rolling well is helpful, but the ability to buy other players' dice is important as well.

I think what impresses me about this game is the way that it feels very much unlike most other games out there.  I think it's that (at least at this point in my limited experience) the best way to earn points is not immediately apparent.  It feels a little bit like a chore in figuring out what the best engine will be.  That's probably due in large respect to the randomness of the cards that come out, but I think that creates an uncertainty that feels fairly unique.

RATING: 6.5
Essen Copy. Interesting dice game, but a bit on the complicated side. (Updates to follow)

[Update]
This is a brain-burner. Because your decision tree is made by picking combinations of dice, there's no real way to make any long term plans. You strategize based on what dice rolls are on the board.

Its definitely enjoyable if you like puzzles. But I think for most non-gamers, this game would be too complicated to be worth teaching to.

RATING: 9
Includes the Expansions:
Troyes: Bonus Cards (First edition) (2011)
Ladies of Troyes: (English first edition) (2012)

I've played Kingsburg and Alien Frontiers, but Troyes beats those games hands down. I don't understand why this one is so much fun, given all the dice rolling and random bad things that dominate the game. Maybe it's the fact that in the midst of all that randomness you have an intense resource management thing going on, for the sole purpose of supporting the worker placement part of the game. And it all comes together beautifully to create a game that is amazingly addictive and fun. Another thing I love about Troyes is the graphic design of the game... except for the title font on the cards, which is often difficult to read. But that is a minor quibble. Troyes is simply excellent!

RATING: N/A
In Troyes, recreate four centuries of history of this famous city of the Champagne region of France. Each player manages their segment of the population (represented by a horde of dice) and their hand of cards, which represent the three primary domains of the city: religious, military, and civil. Players can also offer cash to their opponents' populace in order to get a little moonlighting out of them—anything for more fame!

RATING: 8
Great visual appeal and wonderful use of action dice.  I particularly like the dice manipulation mechanisms in this game, as well as the ability to purchase your opponents' dice.  One of my favorite Euro designs.  It has all of the elements that make Euros interesting to me: hidden information, unknown future events that can radically alter the game state, and a high degree of player interaction.  Of course, most Eurogames lack these elements, as many fans of the genre prefer games with more perfect information and predictability.

The ability to purchase other players dice results in a tense tactical game, with the long term strategy of secret goals offering intrigue and cohesion." - said better than I by BGG User josefsalien

RATING: 9
It's been a while since a game of this caliber came out.  Troyes uses a dice mechanics reminiscent of Kingsburg combined with simple worker placement and variable actions with high interactions, possible combinations and potential synergy.  The result is a fairly deep game with nice strategy, decent player interaction but no attrition and a very limited but valuable random component.  Can take a game to get into for neophytes but was pretty much instant for me.  The variable actions pretty much guarantee that you will need to play *a lot* before this ever feels remotely repetitive.  This will hit the table.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
LIKE:

* Innovative dice mechanic - each player rolls dice according to which region/color they have influence.  However, players are able to buy dice from other players meaning that you may not be able to use the dice that you roll! 
* Action cards bring a ton of variety and replayability to the game as you only use 9 each game and there are 25 available.  Each one allows players who place an influence on the card to use dice in creative ways.
* Each player receives a secret VP requirement for the end game that one should seek to deduce what other players may have.
* First player must always combat the invaders and black dice using their own personal dice.  Assuming the first player only takes out one black die and others are present, each player in turn must defeat at least one black die using their own dice until none remain.
* Cards often provide interesting combos that work well together.
* Although I know some people hate this aspect of the game, I find the fact that you can buy dice from your opponents a very interesting part of this game.  You may think you own the dice, but you may not end up being able to use them if other players buy them out from under you!

DISLIKES:

* The math for buying dice can be confusing initially as the price for each die you buy is based on whether you end up with 1,2 or 3 dice. 
* The game relies on players working together to keep opponents in check.  One person refusing to do this will at times lead to runaway winners by whomever gets the best combo going first.  While I don't mind this in a game if people are aware of this and play accordingly, playing this when people know this and refuse to do anything about it is very aggravating.
*  Turn order is an issue, particularly in a four player game.  If I play with three, we usually play 6 rounds so everyone is start player twice.  However, playing with four is more difficult - I've thought about trying to either go 8 rounds (not sure I want to add another hour to the game) or just going four rounds (not enough time to really get your card combos going.  In short, this seems to be perfect for three and going 6 rounds and less so for other player counts.

Dropped rating from 9 -> 8 after a couple plays where one person ran away with the victory both times because everyone stood around and let him and then blamed the game.  I'm not sure this game is going to get a fair shake around here.

RATING: 8
For a game with piles of dice, random events, and random action spaces there is sure a ton of control in this game.

The key is that no player is ever locked out of being able to use a given action space. There's even the ability to "flip" dice to their opposite face, reroll dice, or action spaces to convert the color of dice.

In all quite a fine game that I hope to own some day.

RATING: 10
Who would have thought that a game which relies so heavily on dice could have so many interesting decisions?  This is a "dice-placement" worker-placement game which obsoletes Kingsburg.  There is not only a lot more going on, but so much more re-playability.  A very in-your-face euro-game with an unusually high amount of interaction.  Not dependent on rolling dice well, because of the myriad of ways that one can spend influence to modify pips, or spend money to buy an opponents dice.  Brilliantly done.

The artwork on the cards and board are timeless, reminiscent of orthodox Russian icons. The symbols used on the cards take a little bit of study to understand, but less nebulous than "Race for the Galaxy" symbology (which I also enjoy).

There are a lot of little, but important, rules in this game.  Some of which seem a bit fiddly.  Small subtle ones that are easily missed, but make a big difference.  I've decided that the game is not in fact fiddly, as in Agricola-fiddly.  But I do recommend playing your first game with an experienced player who can assist in this regard.  Our 4-player games take a while (2-2.5hrs) because we make sure that the activity cards are thoroughly understood by all before moving forward with a turn, and we don't play Troyes often enough to memorize these.  TTFN.

With BGG's 4-card promo.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A terribly written rulebook meant that I made several abortive attempts to learn the rules before I finally got this game to the table. Here's a big tip for rulebook writers: start with a quick rundown of the basic shape of the game before going into details, never introduce new terminology without first explaining it, and when there are cards that do more than one thing, include a diagram labeling the different features of the card.

Rant aside, this is a cool game. The available actions vary from game to game, which promises plenty of variety in terms of strategies used. In theory, this should mean we don't get to see the same one or two strategies used every game (which is a huge flaw in lesser games such as Kingsburg), but only time will tell.

I like the artwork in this game quite a bit, although the fine detail in the line drawings can distract from the iconography on the game-related features of the board. This is a minor problem with form over function, but shouldn't be a problem in a well-lit room.

RATING: 8
Innovative game, with a lot more weight and depth than the rules would suggest.  Don't be deceived by the dice rolling, the game provides ample opportunities to override the element of luck and replace it with tactical decisions.  Very interactive, good playlength, definitely a meaty game that deserves repeated plays.  It gives only the illusion of a lower barrier to entry than similarly weighty games, I have now noticed many times that it takes time for new players to start grokking what happens in game due to its originality and weight.  A nice addition to broaden game choices when you have 90-120 minutes gap and a desire for something heavy that does not require a heavy rules explanation or refreshing the rules every time.  Great components and presentation.  Another good game by Pearl games.

RATING: 8
A very appealing mixture of strategy, tactics and reading your opponents, with a bit of luck thrown in for good measure - enough luck to blame your defeats on cursed dice, but certainly not enough to wash out the other aspects. It’s quite astonishing that such a dizzying heap of mechanisms and action cards and different currencies manages to play quite smoothly, and this might be Troyes biggest accomplishment.

There was a distinct whiff of first player advantage in the two games I’ve played so far, however: in both cases someone with two start player rounds won. I really feel that the game would have benefited immensely from another development round purely aimed at decreasing or increasing the number of rounds in a way that each player gets the same number of start player rounds. A possible fix might be different amounts of starting money or, for experienced players, bidding off the first two positions.

Otherwise, highly recommended and even a possible buy for me.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is one of my favourite games and has been in my top 10 games since I first got it. Lovely crunchy, thinky, tense Euro with dice! One of the best games I have with dice, or without dice, for that matter. 

In Troyes the value (pips) of the dice you have influence over represent the relative strength/influence/work of your workers (councilmen or ladies) that you have placed in different areas of city life (religion, military, civic) but unfortunately nothing in the game is safe as you can buy the dice from each other and bugger up each others plans. But the more powerful those dice become for you the more you have to pay, leading to the player you bought them from to then have a ton of money they are looking to throw about and offload on someone else as they buy their dice in turn. By one (ill conceived or magnificently planned) purchase you can create a veritable tsunami of dice movement and then before you know it the round is over and you are left with a few pips on a yellow dice that you can only trade in for agricultural work and a bit of money. Could be a post modern impression of the workings of the E.U.!

I love every single one of the myriad of mechanics in this game, they all work wonderfully well together. So far every game of it has been different and as a bonus (if you like that sort of thing) the official solo rules are ok as well.

There is nothing bad I can say about this game ... stop don't even raise the subject of the artwork! I think it is wonderful and one of the best examples of fantastic thematic artwork I own in a game ... so don't even go there.

The game is so good that despite it being one of my favourites since I first played it, I haven't even thought about getting the expansion. For me I just imagine it would add to much and end up detracting from the game. But having said that I do like the idea of the purple dice no-one can steal from you ... it would be nice to have just a little bit of security in this game! 

If you don't have this and like thinky euros and fancy adding a twist to your usual gaming cocktail, jump on the reprint as soon as it comes out this summer. I wouldn't grab the expansion straight away though I would recommend trying the base game in its full glory before you going messing around with the Ladies of Troyes!

RATING: 8
It took me 90 minutes to teach myself Troyes, an hour to teach it to my group, and then two hours for our first play -- so this is like nothing else in my collection. It's the deepest game I own, which isn't a bad thing.

I love it.

There's a LOT going on in the game, but the iconography is brilliant, the rules are clear and logical (I only missed one, albeit an important one: the fact that characters apply to everyone), and after a few turns it starts to click. On a second play, I'm confident the same group would hit the listed 90-minute play time. (Unfortunately, 1/4 of my regular group hated it, so it's not likely to hit the table often.)

I love the artwork, the mood, the use of dice -- man is that a brilliant mechanic! -- and the sheer amount of stuff that's packed into Troyes, yet which avoids seeming overwhelming on any given turn.

There's lots of strategy, a nice amount of tactics, and relatively little downtime. I could usually plan my next turn in advance, then juggle it accordingly if someone torpedoed it.

All in all, I'm amazed at how well this comes together -- it's exactly the game I wanted it to be.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.6
This is my favourite game simply because I enjoy it so much.  It doesn't have the complex strategy of other games, but there are so many options in this game, and there is just something about it that I find so much fun.

I really like dice, but don't enjoy games with a high element of luck. This game has the randomness of dice but very little luck; there are numerous effective ways to use whatever has been rolled, you just have to think. 

The player interaction is indirect like most euros, but it is there in abundance compared to others, and this I consider a good feature.

The game is mainly tactical, but there is strategy involved, and your long term strategy will affect you tactical decisions significantly (this might sound daft but hopefully you know what I mean!).  To play it effectively really means that you should have an overall strategy, but allow yourself to be significanly pushed and pulled but the flow of the game.  Compare this to Puerto Rico where you are pushed and pulled much more such that in reality there is little strategy at all, and something like navegador where the actions of others are unlikely to pull you off your main strategy.

There is so much going on in this game, but all in a good way, there is a tightness of not being able to do all the things you want so you have to prioritise, but not so much so that you feel restricted.

The only real problem is that others in my gaming group don't enjoy this game with the abundance that I do, though they do like it.

RATING: 10
Initial rating based on a three-player game. I can see why people compare this to a Feld game, though it feels much less mechanical to me. I like the heavy tactical play, the variation from game to game in the activity (I/II/III) cards, and the options present for forming dice groups, manipulating their values, and placement thereof. My only knocks are that I think the hidden goals should be public and that each player should go first in an equal number of rounds.

----------

I now understand why the character cards are hidden.  There are only 6 of them, so once you've played a few times and get used to them, that added bit of deduction adds something to the game.

The game is fantastic with 2 and 3 players.  With 4, I think it is too chaotic.  Too much can happen between one turn and the next and you don't get enough dice to start with.  I suppose some might say this is made up for by the fact that there are more rounds in the game, but I don't think so.

My rating is 10 for the 2-player game (it is utter perfection), 9 for the 3-player game and 7 for the 4-player game.  I won't play with 4 any longer.

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Good God, this is organised chaos. If you're the type of gamer who likes to make plans, or who dislikes having other players take their stuff from them; if you dislike the randomness of dice or being screwed by people who bump you from a space just before you take a crucial action then this is not the game for you.

A game of abstract politics, players place their followers to provide civil, military or religious influence. Each follower located in one of the three influence locations gives the player a die - red for military, yellow for civil and white for religious actions. 

Roll your dice and place them in the square. When it's your turn you can use the pips of one colour to provide you with actions and benefits... the trouble is, picking which colour to use can be painful as other players can pay to use your dice. Do you take a military action this turn? If so, you may not get to take that civil action you wanted next turn because the dice may not exist any longer, having been used by an opponent.

Lots of routes to victory. There are cards which give a lot of flexibility and replayability as well as tension. The ways the systems of dice, money and influence interact are simple but clever. There are constant incursions inherent in the system which need to be controlled by the players or they'll lose money/dice/followers. Your opponents can bump your influence - in fact, they can pay to use your own die rolls against you!

An unpredictable, beautiful, glorious mess. Sheer genius.

RATING: 4
I thought this would be a lot more of a engine-builder game, instead it was a tactical game of resource management and thus I liked it a lot better. You have to set yourself up to be able to make the best of any situation. Further plays will most likely reveal certain "must do's" in terms of getting gold from the get go at least, but otherwise I can't see this game falling into set strategies "I'm going for the Red dice strategy" etc. Lovely and cutthroat with wasting other players dice, grabbing spots they need, pushing them out of buildings. A good game!

RATING: 10
This game proves there are exceptions to every rule -- it is full of mechanisms that normally turn me off. It's dice-based, it has secret scoring, even an event deck! But everything comes together in a wonderful way. This is the game for people who think of euros as Agricola-like solitaire garbage. The interaction is woven into the gameplay beautifully, not tacked on. The variety of activity cards gives the game high replayability. It's great at every player count, though 4-player games can run a bit long. The artwork fits the (albeit thin) theme and really grows on you. No game engages me like Troyes.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

One my my new favorites. Fun to play, a bit confusing to learn, but very simple once you figure out the system.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This game takes dice management to the next level.  The board has 6 spots each for military, religious and civil influence.  Players who have workers in these areas will get to roll dice of that color on their turn.  After each player rolls all their dice they're placed on the table and used to pay for actions but first they must fight off any enemies (black dice) and events.  This is where the fun begins.  Besides the dice themselves there are two other currencies: influence points and money.  Players can pay these to flip dice, steal dice, re-roll dice etc.  On a players turn he can try to displace others in one of the dice spots or pay for one of the many cards available - which give special powers, money, influence, etc.  You can also use dice to defeat enemies and earn victory points. 

Overall, it's a very engaging game with a lot of interaction and some meaty tactical decisions.  The dice and their values are used in a variety of ways.  One of the better games to come out in 2010.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

[size=10][u][i][b]r{Initial Thoughts}r (after 5 plays):[/u][/b][/i][/size]
* Unique dice/worker placement medium-weight Euro

If you have a small gaming library, this game is for you, as it really rewards repeated play, but for me, the composition of the game is too much on the heavy side, which is a shame, as the game itself is actually not that hard.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Troyes is built on an intricate resource conversion system of money, influence and victory points. What makes it stands out from other games is its use of dice that are acquired through worker placement and rolled each turn to determine their power. However, they can be purchased by other players so much of the game challenge is about which dice to select and when to avoid that someone else takes them first.

Knowledge of card combinations may be too important but since all cards are displayed to all players, I don't expect this to be a major issue.

All in all, Troyes is a game that stands out with both strategic decisions (how do I obtain the resources I need to progress?) and tactical decisions (which dice do I need to use how and when?).

Update: Sold because was I worried that knowledge of card combinations are more important than knowing when and where to use dice.

RATING: 6.25
Trop compliqué pour rien, trop de cartes différentes, trop de A vers B pour C visant D, trop d'éparpillement, trop de dés, et trop de vols de dés!

Je reconnais que c'est un jeu de qualité, très travaillé. Mais il s'adresse principalement à un groupe d'habitués. Et personnellement, je n'ai pas envie d'en faire partie.

EDIT 2015-06-28: Les commentaires précédents ont été écrits après trois parties jouées. Or, il s'avère que nous jouions en appliquant mal la règle "acheter un dé à un adversaire". Au lieu de calculer le prix d'achat en fonction du lot éventuellement utilisé, nous payions les dés adverses selon la formule "1 dé acheté = 2$; 2 dés achetés = 4$ chacun).

Après avoir rejoué avec la vraie règle, je constate que le vol de dés est un peu moins fréquent. Mon appréciation du jeu (6.2) est par conséquent revu à la hausse (7).

RATING: 9.8
A master piece. There's no game better than this after having played hundreds of Euros.

Everything about it is neat.

- It's very deep. 
- You have a lot of meaningful choices to make in the game. You have a lot of freedom and room for smart and creative play thanks to the dice manipulation and how you can pay for using your opponent's dice
- It's tactical by nature, just like other worker placement game, you need to react according to your opponent's moves
- It's also strategical, as cards that turned up remains face up on the table. You can spend time to craft an engine and reap the rewards when the timing is right
- Unlike the top classic Euro (like Caylus and Puerto Rico), the initial card combination makes every game a refreshing experience
- It doesn't even take long! 60-90 mins playing time, filled with meaningful decisions

RATING: 8
In 1200 France, one places their citizens in the bishopric, palace or city hall. This in turn gives you the corresponding colored dice to roll. During your action phase, you use the dice, and you can buy dice from other people, to put tradesmen on cards, counter events, put more citizens on the board, earn money or construct the cathedral. The three different colored dice for your citizens restricts where you can use the dice. 

Only certain activity cards are used per game, so each game will be different. 

In addition, each player is dealt a secret character card, who rewards VPs for doing certain things, which all players can earn. 

I'm looking forward to seeing more of the card combinations, and other strategies. I'm at the point where I realize the secret character cards can swing the game one way or another, without too much of your control, but I like the aspect of the dice buying and thinking about seeding of the citizens in the beginning. There are lots to think about, which I like. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Very unique game. Worker placement, resource management and dice game elements. 2-4 players. 

PROS:
-Variable game setup each time. 
-Low luck as the dice can be modified in various ways and you also buy other players die to use for your own purposes.
-Dynamic game because of the dice rolls. 
-Good player interaction: buying the dice, pushing their workers off board locations.  

CONS:
-The differing crisis cards can be very devastating, especially to some players if unable to defeat it. 
-Is a definite advantage to go first, which varies based on player number and how many turns the game goes for.
-Still a little bit of luck, if its your turn to go first and you roll many 6s, that's great! if you are last and roll many 1s too bad!
-Didn't like the hidden lord cards. If hidden, should only apply to that particular player. As it applies to all players, would prefer to have them face up.

RATING: 9
Troyes is this close to being another soulless Euro, this time with dice and events a la Kingsburg, worker placement a la Caylus, and passive aggressive sissy-fights a la Hansa Teutonica.  What separates this game from total pastiness is the built-in timer of the dicepool (which forces your decision to become tougher as the pool stays wide, but quickly makes your decisions for you) and the secret end-goal characters (which do a lot to add theme to the game and diversify the play experience for players).  And the artwork, which is fantastic in a low-key way.

Man, how I prefer Troyes to Hansa Teutonica.  It out-Felds Feld and yet it is 50 times more attractive to me than 90% of Feld's library.  I shouldn't like it as much as I do.  But I adore this game.

The expansion, however, is a fucking insult.

RATING: 9
Troyes is a dice game where the dice are purchased with cash, then spent to gain victory points and setup scoring and resource mechanisms. The dice are available from a shared resource pool, but players may have more or less access to the pool due to turn order or lack of cash. Troyes has variable setup because different cards will come into play each game.

The components are good, although the English rules are difficult. There are also green and orange player pieces, which is useless for players with red-green colourblindness.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
I'm not the biggest fan of medium-heavy Euros insofar as rules complexity goes. I do love a good elegant Euro or Knizia, but some of these "point salad" type games have at least as many exceptions and rules oddities as the greatest Ameritrash offenders. But all in all learning this was a pleasant experience.

I do like the idea of not "owning" particular dice in the workforce, but simply getting a discount on yours. I love the event deck, the solo rules, and the thematic elements. I know it's JASE in a sense, but most of the actions have at least some semblance of theme to them. There is a high degree of connectivity among the rounds, so you need to make sure to set yourself up to have enough dice and coins later on in the game.

I am curious whether this will have legs after I've become proficient at cutting through the crap and figuring out what actions are worthwhile. I am hopeful that the hidden character cards will help with this.

In the end it loses its luster and turns into just another optimization game.

RATING: 8
[b]Troyes[/b]

[i][COLOR=#FF0000]A year later... thoughts and rating (8 -> 8)[/COLOR][/i]

[i]I haven't had the opportunity to play this again, but I would like to. If I do I will update my thoughts.[/i] 

[COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (8):[/COLOR]

[i]I played this early in the month and to be honest I'm having trouble remembering specifics of the game. This is an extremely dry Euro, so if that's not your thing I wouldn't recommend it. I still enjoy these types of games and really enjoyed figuring out how to play this game well. The way the dice pool works adds interaction to the game since someone can always pay you to use your dice. While that sounds like a decent deal you can get truly hosed if you end up not being able to use the dice you need that turn (each group of actions is tied to one of the 3 die colors). Add in the area control element found on the board and you have a really tight, engaging optimization puzzle on your hands.

What makes Troyes stand out is the player interaction combined with the end game hidden scoring objective each person has. You can plan all you want but someone can recognize what you are doing and come in and buy your dice, or bump you out a critical space you need to score your end game objective. You need to pay attention to what other people at the table are doing or you will not be successful. Yet another game I played this month that I really want to get back on the table.[/i]
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
[q="Morganza"]What did you think of Troyes? [/q]

It wasn't very fun. As prominent games go (i.e. ignoring all the dreck that make up the bulk of the database) I would put it about average - about half games are better, half are worse. Maybe a little below average, say 5/10. I'm not keen to play it again and would actively avoid it and lobby for something else. It's not a 'bad game', just not my cup of tea. I don't like worker placement games. I've not found a definitive reason [i]why[/i], just every worker placement game I have played has bored. I'm engaged the whole time and thinking hard, but I'm not getting any enjoyment out of it. Other meaty games like say 18xx, Puerto Rico, Brass etc. have me thinking hard [i]and [/i]enjoying the game. 

Troyes in particular (which I found out is not pronounced anything like I expected - taught by someone who speaks French) is very much a 'cube pusher', only it's not always cubes being pushed, they might be cardboard tokens or a marker on track or something. Players are supposedly tasked with building a cathedral, by setting up a Rube-Goldberg style chain of interactions that trades prestige for meeples, for dice, for cubes, for money, for cards, for VPs, (or something like that) that hopefully magnifies their resources at each point along the chain.  :snore:  

It's certainly got one or two clever mechanics like buying dice off each other (we only played 2 player, I suppose there'd be a lot more of that going on with 4 or 5 or however many it goes up to), and the meeples giving you more dice to roll (the dice are the primary workers to be placed, not the meeples), but I felt there were a lot of mechanics shoehorned in for no discernible reason.

RATING: 8.5
While most Euro, dice-centric games mandate a certain "blindness" to other player's turns, Troyes made me constantly take note of what other players were doing...then I just took that away from them on my turn. There is an amount of spite generated from "stealing" someone's better die results and "pushing" someone's meeple out of a building but that's natural in a game that encourages interaction and semi-cooperation. The artwork is great and there is re-playability with different character cards, tradesmen and events. Solid game and always a pleasure to play.

RATING: 7.5
Had a lot of hype, and I still thought it was pretty cool. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
You set up to earn different coloured dice, where each colour will allow you to take different actions, the number generally driving how many times you can do that action. Which sounds random, but you can use your influence points to manipulate the dice and you can use your money to steal other people's dice, so now you have lots of options, which generates plenty of downtime. Except there's only so many things you can spend them on - get more dice, get vp's, get influence. Each of the first 3 rounds adds some extra actions to invest in (card driven, driving some intra-game variety) but other than that, each round feels pretty much the same. So you feel like you've just played the game 5 times within 90 minutes. That's it, roll the dice, and then go round and round analysing and spending them. At end of game, see who did it all best when all the numerous ways of earning VPs get added up. There's precious little link to theme. It's quite hitty in a Hansa Teutonica type way, killing off people's future dice, hitting them back in return, stealing dice, stealing in return, and that's a nature I don't enjoy in a themeless cube-driven Euro. The other issue I don't like is that you aren't guaranteed a return on your investment. For example, you invest in a yellow building to convert money into VP's, then you find all the yellow dice are used for other things before you even get a go, making your investment useless this turn, and then you're looking for less optimal means of earning VPs which means hitting other people for dice, and so the merry go round goes. Anyway, it hits all the wrong buttons for me.   
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Troyes is an extraordinary game.  It has a lot in common with some of my favorite games - Trajan, Le Havre, and Aquasphere.
1) It is highly tactical and reactive, like Trajan.  Following a strategic path feels like La Havre, in that getting from point A to point B in your plan takes longer is and more complex than it initially seems.  But it doesn't shower you with points like Trajan or Le Havre.  It is tight like Aquasphere and has you feeling like you are wiggling and and eeking out points.  Average end-game scores are around 40-mid 50s.
2) It can feel like a head to head struggle where you and your opponents are jockeying for advantage throughout the entire game (once everyone knows how to play)
3) It is a game that you can explore.   You can't oversimplify the game into "just do this strategy and you will win".  You can't "break" the game.  The game is very rich, and complex, and there are many subtle things that involved in decision-making that affect the point struggle.
4) Although similar in feel to Trajan, Troyes' gameplay mechanism of buying dice from other players is very original. Additionally,   all of the things you can do in this game interconnected - and giving any one of them attention effects the others, as well as your opponents.   For these reasons, Troyes stands out as very unique in my collection.

Caveat:  This is a Eurogamer's Eurogame.  Very thinky, and many would find it boring and themeless - like some say about the games I compared it to.  I would say if you like those games a lot, then this would be right up your alley.  For me it fires on all cylinders and it ranks high among my favorite games.

RATING: 4
What I liked:
- Components, except that the icons should have been more consistent.
- Rolling dice is usually fun.

What I didn't like:
- High complexity. It felt like it was complex just to be complex, and not because it added more to the game.
- Prone to AP, partly because there are too many ways to manipulate the dice.
- You definitely need to learn the cards to use the combos.
- Having each player know one of the end-game scoring bonuses, which means bluffing (which I don't like in games).

The 3.4 weight seems about right. This game is going to appeal to people who like complexity in general.

RATING: 9
La vera sorpresa dello Spiel 2010. Un gioco a lungo atteso per il suo utilizzo originale dei dadi e per l'ottima veste grafica (che fa onore a un giovane editore come Pearl Games).
Il fattore sfortuna è paradossalmente limitato: ogni risultato del dado può essere utile, e se non lo è si può sempre ritirare (o capovolgere il risultato). Ci sono un sacco di cose da fare: dalle minacce esterne, alla costruzione della cattedrale, al piazzamento dei propri commercianti presso le personalità della città, all'occupazione dei palazzi di Troyes. 
L'assegnazione dei punti dei personaggi nascosti non è un ulteriore bonus, ma un vero e proprio salto: occorre quindi tener d'occhio il comportamento degli avversari (che conoscono un obiettivo a testa) per capire dove muoversi.
Un gioco estremamente originale e divertente. Chapeau.
