RATING: 2
I had a bad initial reaction to Troyes.
In part it was due to a rule error.
So I thought that I ought to give it another try.
Well, I just have: as a two player game.
Positive conclusion: it is a better two player game than four player.
Negative conclusion: I don't want to play it again with any number of players, especially not with more than three.

My problems with it: I have no problem with a pasted on theme if it it done well. Sometimes the results are amateur DIY but Troyes joins Trajan as a game where the theme has slid down on to the floor.
This means that I am performing actions that I don't care about and it needs too much thought to be a light, fun game and outstays its welcome.
The dice add luck instead of randomness: not enough dice are rolled, especially with more players, and the effects of being first or last with bad dice can be decisive, especially when there are fewer building slots to occupy. Fundamentally the game does not scale well.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Have played once in a two-player game.  I tend to like these kinds of "victory point engine" games, and Troyes has some clever, enjoyable mechanics that rarely leave you with nothing at all to do on a turn, and even dropping out of a turn early can get you a benefit.

After a couple plays, I think I just really dig this system.  There are slight nuances to the ways to acquire points, and a few picky rules, but, as I said above, there's almost always SOMETHING you can do on a turn, even on the cheap, and though a lack of money or a lack of influence are handicaps, there are almost always ways around it.  It also rewards clever play, and most certainly long-term play.

RATING: 8
I usually hesitate to rate games after 1 play but for me it was clear Troyes was the best game I have played to come out of Essen 2010 and I wanted to give it the credit it deserves.

Great replayability--not only do dice rolls change from game to game but the action spaces and events do as well.

Tough decisions--it's not clear what the best choice is, at least not initially. You have to play from your gut, it's impossible to 'solve'.

Player interaction--buying other players' dice prevents them from being able to carry out their plans but comes at a steep price.

I want to play it again soon as I think this will help me understand better why I enjoyed this game so much,

RATING: 6
Another money-cube-VP worker placement engine game with multicolored dice thrown in. Nothing distinguishes it from many other with the same complexity, some of which is unnecessary.

The 4-player game (3 1st-timers) took 30+ minutes to explain and 2.5 hours to play. Final scores were 47 (1st player with previous experience), 42, 35, and 31 VP.... in player order. It is unfair that the 1st 2 players were first twice, the others only once. This is because the cute dice mechanism strongly favors turn order. Although initially interesting, the dice routine becomes fairly obvious as play goes on which then makes the slow pace, due to no possibility of tactical planning, annoying. Black dice attacks and Cathedral 'building' are just complexity add-ons. Limitless replayability,  by adding dice random elements and a deck of arbitrary (theme-less) cards, does not add up to elegance.

RATING: 7.5
I can honestly see where this game is coming from. The first dice allocation game I played was Kingsburg which is probably the most simple of the bunch. I played Alien Frontiers and did not like the theme and found it a tad more complex than the former. Troyes however is the behemoth of the trio and takes the dice allocation mechanic and beefs it up with lots of stuff, rules and tweaks. If you are a fan of the genre, that is, someone who looks for more of the same in a heavier context, Troyes is the go-to game for you. However, I still prefer Kingsburg because the game keeps it simple, focuses more on interaction instead of calculations and brainburning. Troyes takes it one level to far (for me) but I can see why some people might really like it. I am not keen on the fact that you get an advantage by learning the cards by heart either, I do not like that, I want my games to be decided by skill, not by sheer investment of time into preparing for it beforehand.

RATING: 6
Boy, this game can really give you a headache. There are options aplenty in every single turn and you really need to be on your toes, because situation can change in the blink of an eye. 

"Troyes" is a very tactical game about mediev... about using random cards (but common for everybody) to create the most efficient engine. There are several things you have to manage: money, influence, points, character tasks and so forth. By using the dice, you activate cards to utilize various effects. Sounds simple? It's not!

I think this one could be almost on par with other dice games I love, particularly those made by Mr. Feld, but it's too convoluted. And there's another big complaint: it's very abstract. The symbols on the cards is all that matters. Theme? Non existent. I could easily list all the important card abilities, but I have no idea what they represent, never bothered to check. Used to enjoy it, but I begin to think there are better medium weight eurogames out there.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
DEEP DICE BASED RESOURCE MANAGEMENT GAME
One of those games you have to play to understand.  Dice as a resource to be managed and you have the option to "buy" your opponents dice, manipulate your own dice by expending other resources.  Interlocking operation of dice rolls, influence points, money, and victory points.  At first blush it sounds random, dry, and confusing - which may be your experience for the first couple of turns of play - but it all interlocks beautifully in a very engaging way.  I suspect I will increase my rating with subsequent plays.  This is a dice game that many a euro dice hater will embrace.  [11-10-11]  Also, one of those rare games that shines with 3 players.  My only real complaint with Troyes is that it suffers from a more serious than usual for a Euro case of the theme being COMPLETELY detached from the gameplay - the excellent and evocative artwork is wasted in this regard.   

PSSSST - ALIEN FRONTIERS IS BETTER!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
When I first played this game, way back in 2011 when I was relatively new to gaming, I thought it was a dull-as-ditchwater dry Euro that was just about saved from utter awfulness due to the hidden character cards which determine end-of-game scoring. But when coming back to it a year or so later, and being much more receptive to Euros in general than I was at first, I saw lots to admire here. Different each time due to the cards chosen for the game (which you don't know until they're revealed in the appropriate round), several different resources to juggle as in any good Euro (chiefly money and influence, but also of course the dice themselves), and never any shortage of hard choices. It's also quite unlike every other Euro I know. Tends to be fairly low-scoring (perhaps we're all still terrible at it, but typical scores seem to be in the 30-40 range), so every single VP you can eke out can make a big difference. It is a good game - both interesting and fairly unique, with no shortage of tough decisions - yet in the final analysis it is difficult for me to love, and I can't remember the last time I felt like I wanted to play it.

RATING: 7.5
A good and interesting game, although the rules and mechanics take some getting used to, even experienced gamers have been seen asking "hang on, how does that bit work again?" but once you start getting a feel for it, things become much simpler.

There are many routes to victory, the trick is combining your dice (and those bought from others) with the cards that allow you to perform various actions (boosting die rolls, converting money to VP or influence, buying dice for free etc) and since these cards change every game what worked last time may not work this time!

If you like heavy Euro Gaming combined with dice and worker placement, this game will probably be right up your street.

RATING: 7.7
(+) Original mechanism
(+) Lots of variety and replayability due to different actions every time you set up the game
(+) I hate dice games, but in Troyes the dice-mechanism is implemented very elegantly
(+) The game does not outstay its welcome: it plays rather quickly and smoothly for a game with quite a serious 'depth'.

(-) Depending on the card actions, the combination of dice rolls and initial starting player there can be quite some randomness in the game. This is also the case in how certain 'event'-cards play out at the beginning of each round.
(-) I like the original mechanism, but to some players the game might feel a bit 'dry'.

Approx gametime: 25 - 30 min / player

RATING: 8
In a 4 player game, there are 6 rounds. At the beginning of each round, all players are defending the city of Troyes from outside marauders by working together. Immediately after, all players turn on each other in a ruthless attempt to have control of each of the 3 buildings (Commerce, Military, Church) in the city. There is tension on every turn in this game. That's what makes it a great game.

RATING: 7
As dice manipulation games go, where you pick a couple of dice from a set and perform an action based on the combination of pips showing, it is the heaviest and the best. Paradoxically, there's relatively little luck involved and a better player will almost always win. I don't like this genre, but will make an exception for Troyes and play it from time to time.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
After further play this comes in at a rock solid 8 for me. A very clever use of dice in a quick Eurogame that rewards further play after you become familiar with the cards, card combinations and actions. If it was a long game I might not rate it so highly, but because it only takes 30 minutes I find it's not overstaying its welcome.

Quite a simple game really. The simplicity allows you to spend your energy evaluating the puzzle that is how best to build your VP engine. The angst comes from the rapidly dwindling dice supply each turn and whether you'll have enough turns and dice to get what you need to get done.

I'm not sure I understand any criticism of this game based on people "stealing" your dice. They don't steal them, they pay for them. Roll good dice and you can anticipate them being bought off you if you wait too long. Build your prestige and pay to convert your bad rolls a little later in the round perhaps. There are wonderful options available that keep you on your toes throughout. You can pay for others or use all your own for free. The escalating dice cost is a beautiful mechanic. It rewards specialisation and turn order timing and the steady building of a plan. Rather than this being annoying I find it to be one of the main attractions of the game. You have to juggle your building spots for the right to roll more dice versus using your turn to generate points or all the other option of where to use the dice. You've always got to be thinking of a plan B and a plan C for generating points and repelling invaders and and and. There are options and planning and a good level of thought. I play games exactly for this sort of interaction. I'm not looking for  solo gaming experience, I'm looking to plan for and react to other players.

The problem I can sometimes see with the game is that more fun will be had on an imaginative path to VP generation, but that won't necessarily guarantee a win over the dull path to VP generation. Allow me to elaborate: Due to circumstance and playing style you'll either be having an entertaining and thought provoking game juggling dice and cubes while you buy up essential dice before others get to them, or you'll have decided on your VP strategy early and the game will devolve into a boring exercise in sameness for you over each round. It's easy to find yourself in a position where you've set yourself up early, nothing better presents itself and you're simply following a solid yet ultimately dull path to VP riches. Now some might argue that the fun comes from deviating from that path slightly and trying to squeeze an extra VP over here and deny your opponent dice they need over there, but even playing this way won't generate enough interest for this player. I think this problem is caused by certain strong VP generating cards coming out early in the game - especially if they're combined with character card objectives pushing you down a certain path (like acquiring event cards).

All in all it's a tight package with excellent re-playability, nice artwork and most importantly a clever and forgiving use of dice that makes it different from the other engine building VP generating games out there. I'm not 100% convinced of the balance of the craftsmen cards but I'm looking forward to further play.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Players manage dice in order to get actions and combos to get victory points. High player interaction and innovative mechanics.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Troyes really caught our house by storm earlier this year. After sitting on my shelf unopened for a few months, I opened it up, and my wife and I subsequently played it 4 times. Excellent game that easily deserves its high ranking.

Troyes is a Euro with dice, but different than Castles of Burgundy or Bruges. The dice represent your workforce, but not really with worker placement. The interaction comes in the form of buying dice from other players, which effectively steals from them. The action options provide different strategies to stack together, and lots of good, balanced win conditions.

This is a great 2-player game that I expect I will enjoy at other player counts, too. I'm really glad I have this one.

(Note: I spent 4 1/2 months of my LDS mission in the actual city of Troyes, a town I love quite a bit, a fact which helps me enjoy the game even more.)

RATING: 4
[2011-08-13]
A 2010-es esseni játékmegjelenések egyik legfelkapottabb játéka a Troyes. Tökéletesen értem, hogy miért, mégis azt mondom nekem ilyen típusú játékra nincs szükségem. Miről is van szó. Egy komoly, mélyen kidolgozott, a sokféle lap miatt változatos mechanizmus mellé kitalálták, hogy mi lenne, ha kockákkal bolondítanák meg. Tehát egy komoly gémer játékot alkottak kockákkal. De hisz ez óriási ellentmondásnak tűnik és sajnos a játék bizonyítja, hogy az is. Nehéz összefoglalni a Troyes-t, de megpróbálom. Az öt körös játékban három helyre 3 kör alatt egy-egy kártya kerül, egy helyen pénzt lehet szerezni az utolsó helyszínen pedig befolyást és győzelmi pontot kaphatunk egy jó kis mechanizmussal. A kártyákon akciók hajthatók végre, amik vagy a kockáinkat befolyásolják vagy különböző módokon befolyás és győzelmi pontokat hozhatnak. A három kártyás helyszínhez egy-egy szín tartozik, amelyeken ott levő munkásaink darabszáma alapján kapunk kockákat a kör elején a megfelelő színben, amivel dobásunk után ezeket felhasználhatjuk. Sőt, pénzért el is orozhatjuk egymás elől. Ezek előtt azonban közös várunkat még a veszélytől is meg kell óvnunk dobott kockáink felhasználásával. Tehát egyrészt meg kell gondolnunk, hogy a 3. körtől 3 helyszín 3-3 lapjai, még két helyszín és az összes megjelent veszélylapka közül hova használjuk el dobott kockáinkat vagy ellenfeleinkét. Ebből is látszik, hogy komoly gondolkodós játék, de ha 1-eseket dob az ember, mert a kis számok a kedvezőtlenek, megdől minden. És ezzel tulajdonképpen az egész játék mivolta megkérdőjeleződik számomra. Persze lehetőség van a kockáinkat újradobni vagy akár átfordítani is, de ezekért befolyáspontokat kell beáldoznunk, ami újabb munkások felhelyezéséhez szükséges, illetve nagyon fontos más dolgokhoz is. Túl értékes és kevés van belőle, hogy erre szánjuk, illetve ha erre költjük, másra nem jut. Az egyik kedvenc játékom az Alea Iacta Est, ami szerintem méltánytalanul alulértékelt játék. Az persze sokkal egyszerűbb, mint a Troyes, de ott minden kockaértéknek, a kicsiknek is fontos felhasználási helyük van. Sajnos nem tudok mit kezdeni a Troyessel, nekem ez a játék, mint már korábban említettem egy órási ellentmondás. Szeretem a kockajátékokat, de eszembe nem jutna mély gémerjátékot képzelni mellé. A tábla kinézete nagyon komor, nem is igazán mondható szépen kidolgozottnak, kártyák nagyon szépek ugyan, de az ikonozással már korántsem voltam kibékülve, persze az díjazandó, hogy nem szöveggel oldották meg őket. A szabálykönyv sem sikerült túl jóra, sőt. Nem véletlen a BGG-n jelentkező sok felmerülő kérdés.

RATING: 5
Troyes is a resource management game with non-obvious strategy. Usually in games like this you build your engine in the first part of the game before running it into the ground just before the game ends. Not so in Troyes, which is much more tactical and far less rewarding of resource hoarding. You don't get points for everything at the end of the game. Also, there's meaningful interaction between players. You have to consider your opponents' plans, otherwise you'll lose.

Troyes is not the kind of game I typically enjoy, and yet I do enjoy it, almost despite myself. It's far from my favourite, but I recognize that it's a good example of its kind. And I'd probably add an extra point to my rating if the icons weren't so unintuitive, a pet peeve of mine.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
It is yet another example of a "hey that's a cool way to use dice" game. After several plays, I'm still not entirely certain that you can compensate for rolling poorly. Still, a fun game.

[b]--- Summary ---[/b]
The board shows several areas, each of which corresponds to a different color of die and have several spots in which to place workers, and most have places for cards of the same color (which also may have workers placed on them). Everyone starts the game by placing some workers on the board, each of which will provide a die of matching color. At the start of each round, roll all your dice (and some neutral if playing with fewer players than the maximum allowed) and place them in the matching player (and neutral) areas on the board. Then, fight the external threat cards sort of like Kingsburg, but sequentially in turn order. Then, in turn order, round and round, either pass (for rest of the round) or take an action. Actions require spending various combinations of dice -- from your pool, or dice you buy from other players' pools. Buying dice, though, is expensive: each die costs 1 coin per total number of dice you're using this action (1 = $2, 2 = $4 EACH, 3 = $6 EACH). The combinations (of specific number and color of die) needed depend on what you're trying to do: placing workers on cards, using actions on cards, recruiting new workers, etc. The actions generally earn you money or VP. After a specific (5?) number of rounds, the player with the most VP wins. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
I love worker placement mechanics but because there are so many good worker placement games out there I'm perhaps more scrupulous with them than other games and mechanics.  This one is a winner because it does a lot of things that feel new.  Unlike Alien Frontiers it also melds dice rolling and worker placement in a way that works and makes for interesting strategic and tactical decisions.

The game has a few different phases that make up an entire game round.  First a new activity is revealed for the first three rounds.  Then players acquire money and subtract from that wages based on how many dice they get to roll that turn.  Then they roll dice which will determine which actions they can take that round.  Then the game slaps all the players around a little bit with an event before they can actually take actions.  Finally, the players take their actions.

The coolest mechanic in this game is the ability to buy dice from other players.  This reminds me a little bit of the work contract mechanic from Ora et Labora and I loved it in that game as well.  As long as a player has the funds to do so they can steal good rolls right out from under you.  It opens the game up a lot and makes for some very interesting decisions and plays.  It almost has you hoping you roll POORLY!

I think what makes this game work when combining dice rolls with worker placement where I felt Alien Frontiers did not work is that the majority of the actions only care about the total value of the dice used.  That value is divided by some number and the results of your actions are calculated based on that simple arithmetic.  This is starkly different from Alien Frontiers which mostly required runs, doubles, triples, etc. to activate spaces.

Also, Troyes has a small amount of variety.  There are a few different activities and events that are randomly chosen each game.  Each player also has a secret role that gives ALL players bonuses at the end of the game based on certain criteria.  This is another great element of the game that has you both trying to accomplish your goals without advertising them too much AND trying to guess what the other players' roles are.

All in all a great worker placement game with some real good depth and options without really being overbearingly complicated.  The variety is a little skimpy for my usual tastes but its a good enough game that the Ladies of Troyes expansion is probably a definite buy in the near future.

RATING: N/A
A solid design with a few new mechanics using dice. What I like in the game is the balance between each of the strategies open to each player but also the relevance of each action you take. Everything seems to matter and there's no space for common think and distractions. What I don't like on the game is almost the same thing: a dice game, full with color but heavy and dry as a desert, meaning that the authors took the easy, relax have fun component usually provided with dice and turned the game into an authentic maths exercise. A gamers game that I need to play more to understand every nuance on the cards because it turned out to be, as the final result showed, a card game moved with dice!

I still have some doubts about the importance of dice result as a winning factor. In a 2 player game it seems that the luck factor can be more significant than with more players.
In the bright side of the game are the dice mechanism and its novelty. On the dark side of the game is the long time strategy feeling (which I don't have in the game) and also the dice! They can be more relavant than I would like them to be. 

UPDATE:
After playing the game a few more times with 2/3/4 players I like it better with 2/3 than with 4 (because it's so much more time consuming and uncontrolled) but then again it's just a small difference, nothing too relevant that I wouldn't like at all.
I still believe that in the 2 player game the difference of results in the dice can be crucial. Maybe because with more players, players can buy dice from each other at reasonable prices without loading the opponent with too much money establishing a huge difference. In a 2 player game if a player buys a better die from the adversary (a few times) he would load his opponent's wallet constituting this a big handicap to himself.

Troyes is nominated for "Jogo do Ano 2010" award by Spiel Portugal (http://www.spielportugal.org/jogo-do-ano/2010/nomeados)

RATING: 9.8
Awesome dice worker placement game (luck is mitigated) with some strategic depth and so many intertwined paths and ways to manipulate things in order to get what you want accomplished.

While working through how you'll manipulate the action cards to get what you want, you always have to be mindful of what dice and values are left so that you know how your opponents can possibly affect you on the next turn and upcoming turns.  Hey may take one die that you planned on using next turn, or he may be able to harm your current board position (kick you out of one of the buildling) depending on what dice are left.

Love the game! One of my top games.

RATING: 10

VERDICT

RATING: 7.5
Troyes soars head and shoulders above the also-ran dice allocation games, the likes of Kingsburg and Alien Frontiers -- though perhaps not Macao -- by simple expedient of making it possible to buy others' dice. It sounds insane, but it works: a lot of the time you won't want "your" dice anyway, so it doesn't matter if someone else has rolled better than you. You just buy theirs. Or, if you roll spectacularly badly, you can spend some influence to make your dice spectacularly good instead.

Of course it's not [i]quite[/i] that straightforward; there's good reason to jockey for dice-workers. Of course there's a luck element -- if the stars align and give you good dice at the right time, you can have a truly spectacular turn. (Or the reverse.)

But Troyes has so many ways to mitigate poor rolls that the dice aren't something you have to dread. I'm used to hating evil dice that occasionally, grudgingly give me something I can use. Here, they're not threatening at all, they just contribute to a uncertain tactical landscape. That, I love.

Also, beautiful artwork.

RATING: 6.25
The glaring flaw I see with Troyes is the randomness and luck. Not in the dice as that is mitigated with the ability to buy other player's dice, but in the action cards that are flipped over. Since the different action cards are face down, it is impossible to predict what the most valuable combo of cards will be. If one player invests heavily in a card that gives bonuses to yellow dice, and it just happens that later on yellow dice are the most powerful cards, they have a huge "first mover" advantage by sheer luck.

The only suggestion to fix this problem would be to play with all action cards face up, just with the ability to only use the cards that would normally be face up (first card for first round). The incoming encounters would still be random, but that is relatively minor compared to some of the game breaking overpowered action cards that can show up on turn 3.

RATING: 8
Not a worker placement but a resource assignment.
Clever mechanic where everyone throws their set of dice at the same time. Then on your turn you use your dice and can buy the dice from any opponent who has what you need.But the more you buy the more it costs: exponentially, so no rampant buying.
The greatness of this game is the Butterfly Effect of slightly different starting conditions lead to radically different games.
Resources are spent in several medieval economy themed places and for the first three rounds a new card for use becomes available at each economy spot(defence/ Attackers/ Religous/ Trade- and a Cash Convertors).
 It is how you use your limited money and turn order to occupy these cards that make each game so different from one play to the next.
90mins to play- but it is full on thought so time flies.
Fantastic game one of my all time faves.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Mikael
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Dice rolling/allocation and worker placement hybrid that isn't quite as clever as it thinks it is. Players begin by placing meeples in one of three sections of the city relating to military, religious and civil developments. If the players' meeples are still in those areas at the start of their turns, they get dice corresponding to each of them, and depending on what numbers they roll the dice can be used to perform different actions. These can range from fighting invaders from outside the city, helping to build the cathedral, or acquiring victory points on certain event cards. There seem to be a lot of decisions on offer at any one time in 'Troyes' but overall players are probably best advised just to go for the victory points when they're available, which leads to an overall impression of the game being deceptively shallow. Nevertheless 'Troyes' does have its merits but it probably lags behind other dice allocation games such as 'Kingsburg' and 'Alien Frontiers' somewhat on account of a lack of diversity in the strategic options it provides.

As a system the game seems to work but as a game I was left cold, just calculating point differentials whilst trying to screw over the other players. No sense of progression, no thematic connection. I love the art but I don't think this will be staying. 
Hidden VPs seems ridiculous in this sort of game and add to the chit count which is always a bad thing.

RATING: 8.5
This is a fantastic game.  I've played it 4 times, and it's gotten better each time.  Although there's a large amount of randomness with the dice rolling, it really doesn't *feel* all that random most of the time.  The ability to manipulate dice & purchase dice from other players adds some strategy.  It's a very player-interactive game, with multiple pathways to gain points. There are a lot of different mechanics going on in this game, which makes it fairly complex.  They flow quite well once you understand how they work together, but I would not recommend this for a starter game, and even casual gamers familiar with things like Settlers or TTR may find it daunting at first.

RATING: N/A
130 zł
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Deep game with good replayability right out of the box. It has a number of proven mechanics which mesh well with each other to create a unique experience.

First, there's dice and dice allocation. Roll dice, allocate them to do stuff. Fun, adds a natural tension (as you start chucking those dice). Good design is that there are no 'bad' rolls, only rolls that fit or don't fit with your current strategy. As a big rule of thumb, high rolls are better but you'll need your lows as well.

Second, random hidden bonus cards. Always work well. The catch here is that it is not your personal bonus, but bonuses for everyone that are only revealed at the end of the game. Clever.

Third, economy of three variables: money, influence, and actions. You'll have to manage all three to acquire VPs. When/how you 'eat' into your own engine to start getting these VPs is a big strategic decision. And there are multiple ways to get those VPs: battling events, building a cathedral, claiming or using action spaces. Which one is right for you depends on the actions, the bonus cards in the game and your opponents.

Fourth, claimable action spaces enabling you to do unique stuff. Or nearly, as one other person gets to share the fun but that's it. But they cost you money and influence so when to 'invest' and when to 'hold off' is a big tactical decision.

Fifth, a track (the events) that results in making the board 'tighter' towards the end of the game, making for a natural tension creep. 

Finally, and this might be the best decision: you can use other players rolls, effectively 'stealing' them for money. This results in some very tactical gameplay around using/stealing dice from others for your own good/their detriment. This can feel as 'nice' or 'cutthroat' as you like: simply need more points for an action? Just buy the highest die in that color available to you. Need to bleed out someone so they can't use their action? Just scoop up whatever they have for some measly gold they can't effectively use anymore. Juggling between those priorities will be a big thing and this, coupled with the hidden bonus card mechanic (so you will want to know where someone is aiming for) makes you pay attention to other players' turns. Which alleviates one big draw-back of most euros: multiplayer solitaire. This game gets rid of the 'solitaire' (unless played as a single player game), which is a very good thing.

Rating is tentative, as I have only played this a couple of times, but I expect it to go up in time. If your gaming group is into Euros, doesn't shun dice and likes to figure out a puzzle this might be very well the game you'll be playing now for years to come. 

Very good design, another hit by Xavier Georges (Carson City, Ginkgopolis some other examples).

RATING: 9
Played once with 3 players and once with 2 players. The 3 player learning game took quite a while to learn the rules and then consider all the options available while coming up with a decent strategy. The 2 player game (each of us had played once) took one hour. This is a great game where there are enough mechanisms to overcome unlucky dice rolls. Beautifully produced, everything just works perfectly. The action cards and event cards add the variability needed to make sure that each game plays out differently. Very neat mechanisms, good tactical options make for a great game and this has all of it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Troyes brings dice to the heavyweight Euro genre with mixed results.  This is a thoughtful, comprehensive design with too much going on.  The card-based diversity is overbroad, and players prone to AP will be stalled by the myriad choices.  The luck of the dice is mitigated in so many ways to preserve strategic gameplay that they almost don't matter. I'd be happy with all of that if the game had a natural rhythm, but this is a very choppy experience.

RATING: 8
This must be a post-Essen craze going on. The game is certainly fine, but not outstanding.

Pros:
- The artwork. Depending on how you look at it, it is just awesome. It is by no means modern, but it never tried to be. The illustrations come fasciatingly close to early medieval drawings and by doing so add to the theme.
- The odds of ever playing in the same constellation are close to none. You have 27 cards, 9 per category. And it's only three activity cards per category that make it into the game. Plus you have different events (although there's only eight in total - eight red ones to be precise. And in a 4-player game almost all of the yellow aand white event cards will enter the game) From replayability point of view it reminded me of Agricola
- there's quite a lot to bear in mind when taking your actions and quite a lot of actions you can take. But this leads me to the first con ...  

Cons:
- actually it feels like the most meaningful decision you take is the first action of the round with all or many dice still available. Every action after feels like a rather obvious choice. Even more so in later rounds when lots of activity cards are already claimed and the dice for other activity cards are not around (or not enough to activate the card even once) I heard the phrase "Oh, what a surprise" quite often yesterday. So with your first action being that crucial, there's tons of one thing. Which is AP, AP and again AP! And you cannot really make plans unless you know what your opponent does. 
- Every game has a predefined phase order, but this one comes across even more repetitve. Roll your dice, fight the war, take actions, roll your dice, fight the war, take actions...I know that point is kind of unfair, but for me the whole game lacks excitement. 
- It feels very static. 

In conclusion I still enjoyed playing this. It's certainly not a bad game, but I guess that every Spiel' needs an underdog that can be glorified afterwards. 
--------------------------------------------------
Edit: I have to correct myself. Among the games with dice-driven mechanics this is an outstandingly clever design. Ratingfrom 6.5 ->8

RATING: 10
I give it an 8 after the first play because I can see so much depth to it. Our first play was a bit more difficult than I thought it would be. After reading the rules, I was sure it would be fairly straight forward--which is another way of saying the rules are VERY well written. I suspect my rating will go up with more plays and the gameplay becomes more fluid. I think this will eventually be in my top 10.

UPDATE: I had such a wonderful experience with Troyes recently that I a had to come make a comment. What I learned is how different one game can be from the next. We played two games. In the first, we had no problems eliminating the event cards--wam bam! They would appear and disappear, not effecting the game much at all. In the second game, it was a different story. Neutrals meeps were pouncing on all city-building positions, and attack cards were all showing double dice and lots of flags to cover up. The game felt like a co-operative fight against the outside events. Every player at the table commented how different the two games were. I think it's refreshing to find a game that offers a legitimate differing experience when so many games offer superficial replayability. What Troyes offers for replayability is NOT superficial.

RATING: 9.25
There's a lot going on in Troyes.

The game goes out of its way to introduce player interaction, which is very welcome in the world of mid-weight Euros. The Character and Action cards offer a ton of variability and the fact that not all of the available actions are known during the first several rounds forces played to alter their strategy - not just tactical decisions - mid-game.

The decision space is fairly tight, but there's nearly always a good move for each die you have in your possession. It's a matter of deciding if you should use those dice NOW, or steal something to help yourself and throw off an opponent first (and hope that your play is still available later in the round).

RATING: 3.5
First Impression: 
It's a euro attempt gone wrong. 

1. Your starting setup can really hurt you and you have 0 information to help you decide better and not enough time to succesfully change strategy once the game starts. And of course that's all because the cards (randomly picked) are unbalanced. (hello archer) 

2. Too much luck for a euro. This game has both dice and randomly drawn cards. Dice results can be used in various ways but still there are good throws, and bad throws. And since you'll throw your dice only 5 times per game, + perhaps a few times more if you pay, or if some other random mechanic kicks in... it's just not enough to balance the luck. Risk is less luck dependent than this game.

All in all the game is dissapointing. Once it's initial freshness wears off, I'm pretty sure it'll find it's way down where it belongs. (edit:I guess I was wrong about that)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Judging by the rulebook and the artwork, I expected this game to be dry and complex. During my first game with two, however, I was pleasantly surprised. The rules fell into place after the first turn, and the gameplay felt sort of relaxed and full of interesting decisions. Sure, it is yet another dice allocation/worker placement game, but one that feels original and fresh due to the common threats that players have to contend with, the ability to purchase dice from other players and the combination of action cards that is different each game. The iconography used on the board and on the cards is consistent and therefore easy to understand. Also, the game is less about strategy and planning ahead, and more about making the best of what is given to you each turn. It feels like it is unlikely to be shut out from victory after a less than optimal turn. And it all works very well with just two players! One of the few gamers' games that my girlfriend really likes. It has the same feel as The Castles Of Burgund, and I love it to the same degree.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Relatively heavy euro worker placement game that uses dice as workers and mitigates luck elements with clever mechanisms that allow use of other player's dice. Typical of a euro, the game presents an engine by which actions can be taken to convert various assets (in Troyes, this is namely money, influence and citizens) into victory points. There's some variation in the cards and secret-identity roles present from game to game which provides variability in game play. 

Although, I find the game incredibly original, gameplay felt relatively fiddly and overlong. Along with the original game play comes the fact that it's somewhat non-intuitive so an actual play round may be needed to teach the game even to seasoned gamers. All this meaning that first plays will likely be much longer than the 90 minutes that are advertised. This is a game that therefore needs a commitment of multiple plays from a group of players - for me however, I'm not certain I wish to commit to it over other heavy worker placement euros I find more compelling, such as Le Havre or Agricola. And when it comes to scratching the dice-worker-placement itch, I'm more compelled to Alien Frontier's straight forward and tense game play.  

I do absolutely love the graphic design and production is top notch.

Bottom line is Troyes is to be played again given the opportunity, but it's no big deal if that opportunity is not immediate.

RATING: 3
[i]"Poor. Likely won't play this again although could be convinced."[/i]

Zero theme, it's numbers all the way down. There's no real arc to the game either beyond the addition of more powerful actions, players never really own anything so it's 5 resources cycling around while occasionally producing a VP.

Planning is next to impossible - there's not even a turn's notice of which cards are coming up, you select your initial dice colours with NO information at all, and if you aren't first it's a crapshoot which dice will be available to you anyway. VPs are hidden but trackable, and the hidden character cards apply to everyone with arbitrary cutoffs.

Overall this game includes so many basic design mistakes I must wonder if I'm missing something, because I don't understand its BGG ranking at all.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The turn order problem in Troyes  is ridiculous. Even if the game "balances" as some on BGG claim (the argument is that the first players lose some dice via fighting which compensates for the benefit of starting twice) the phenomenological experience is deeply and quite negatively impacted. Playing in the third and worse the fourth position is just a lot less fun. Sure, maybe you have the same chance at victory (who knows, but this is what the defenders claim) but getting the dregs of the left over dice reduces your options, and options are what make a game enjoyable. Troyes is a game that made me rethink what I expect when I think about game balance: it can't just be about an equal crack at the most victory points. Balance really needs to include the notion that all the players having an equally compelling and enjoyable experience. This makes Troyes my first game with a different rating based on my own personal turn position per game: If I am first or second in turn order, Troyes is an 8. 3rd or 4th and I'd give it a 4. It really is too bad that the turn order issue is what is, and seems especially bizarre given the evolution of modern game design.

RATING: N/A
Not a big fan of dice placement games. Also, this game is MEAN, which I don't mind actually. Even though the rules for this game were muddled on first play and I spent a third of it not clear on what was happening, my first impression is that it's a straightforward worker placement game where your workers are dice and the various areas of the board/end goals give you points. Castles of Burgundy is the same concept but scoring points is more interesting by comparison, and I don't even like that game. The way Troyes differs is that you can't directly screw your opponents (ie buy their dice, kick them out of various regions on the map etc) but still, not enough to make this one interesting or exciting.

RATING: 6
To call Troyes a dice game is probably a disservice to the game, since it's really a thinky sort of strategy game that just uses dice as a primary mechanism.  It has a lot less luck than one would expect when hearing that the game uses a lot of dice, and the game has enough of an engine-building characteristic to allow players to work toward a grand move around the end of the game.  There's still a significant enough amount of chaos from the way that other players can buy your dice out from under you (the jury's still out if that's a bad or terrible part of the game), but most of the game can be predicted enough to where you won't be shut out of the game because of it.

The game seems a little dry and forced, but I can't deny that it works pretty well.  It just makes me wonder if, over time, I will ultimately just forget about the game, since it doesn't have the same sort of excitement factor that I enjoy when playing games.  We'll just have to see.

3 plays: 4 players

RATING: 7.5
Initially put off by the art and the fact that dice were involved, but after a few tries on BGA (Board Game Arena,) I have a more favourable view towards it. 

Bad dice rolls can be mitigated by careful management of resources such as influence and gold. It is possible to have your strategy to be hosed down by bad rolls but I do not view this any more differently then when someone deliberately blocks your move in other types of games.

I still feel that the 1st player has a distinct advantage but so far it feels that the game has a way of balancing it out, although some might feel this is dependent on the dice rolls.

Some feel that low numbers on dice hamper you but I feel that there are other things you can use the dice for like the placement of meeples on buildings to knock out an opponents piece to strengthen your position in the next round. Also, remember that the higher you roll while is good for you may also result in attracting your opponents and they will buy the dice from you to block you while improving their status. Granted you do get some renumeration but it usually cannot compare with the actions that can be done on the event or activity cards.

The events in the cards and activities feel, to me, quite thematic, but I can understand how other people may feel like its another dry, soulless euro with a medieval theme and cubes. 

The major complaint I have is that I fell that the game length may be a bit short and ends when you are about to chain your decisions together, preventing satisfying combos.

RATING: N/A
In Troyes, recreate four centuries of history of this famous city of the Champagne region of France. Each player manages their segment of the population (represented by a horde of dice) and their hand of cards, which represent the three primary domains of the city: religious, military, and civil. Players can also offer cash to their opponents' populace in order to get a little moonlighting out of them — anything for more fame!
\13\\13\
Make your underlings:\13\
 work on the cathedral\13\
 combat misfortune\13\
 bustle about the city\13\
 and other such tasks that are below your family's stature

RATING: 10
That's it, I've become addicted to Troyes.  Extraordinary, one of a kind game with fantastic artwork and superb dice allocation mechanics. A masterpiece among modern euro games and one of my personal favourites. 

A word of warning though - this is a "gamer's game" and it's definitely not for everyone. New players might be having problems with deciding what to do during their turns - the path to victory is not obvious and finding an effective strategy among all the available choices can be really challenging.

[i]I own the following expansions: The Ladies of Troyes, 4 Bonus Cards.[/i]

We are rich noble families in this Medieval French city trying to pull it out of economic despair. Each player has a secret identity with end goals. Paths of victory are civilian life (yellow), military (red) and religion (white). Work force represented by rolled dice of each color that can be hired for actions. Game ends after four ages. Need to manage your renown with your actions.  Too much going on for our tiny attention deficit oriented minds.

RATING: 3
The path to victory:  As early in the game as possible, identify one of the degenerate card combinations on the board and run in endless victory point circles until the game ends.

In my case, starting on round 2 or 3, I literally repeated the same two or three dice actions over and over again until I won, which was fun for absolutely no one at the table.  Moreover, others did exactly the same thing; it just happened that my broken VP combo was a tiny bit better than theirs, as were the hidden endgame VP goals that I lucked into accomplishing.

I'd sooner cut out my tongue than ask to play this again.

RATING: 7
So, it's essentially themeless, which is usually a big negative for me, but for some reason I liked this one pretty well.  I think it's because the dice add in enough uncertainty to keep things interesting.  You have several options, but not so many that it's overwhelming.  Players can affect each other, but not overly much.  The game moved right along and it's only 5 turns long.  It's quite tactical because even though you are in some sense building up for the big scoring opportunities at the end, you don't know what they're going to be until the game is half over.  If I remember right, the score was something like 43-43-41, but I don't remember who was tied for the win.  Maybe that's a bad sign, but I enjoyed the game while it was going on and I'd play it again.

RATING: 7.5
Poster child for games that get better with repeated plays. Second play went much smoother than first, down to about 90 minutes for 3-players.

Takes dice allocation and worker placement to another level. Very interesting and a pretty good brain burner. AP might be somewhat of an issue here. Find the building combos and hit them until the VP machine dies. Use of neutral dice also allow some wiggle room for actions.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
It was a lot of fun, but I get the impression that it's a game that's almost always going to be very close... between its hyper-balanced Euro nature and the ability to hurt the leader, the only way the game won't be decided on the last turn is if someone isn't really paying attention to the score and is inadvertently kingmaking by hurting the second-place player instead.

In those sorts of games, it's hard to feel like the rest of the game has any significance; someone has an advantage coming into the last turn, perhaps, but someone different would have had one if the game had ended one turn earlier or later.

Hiding the victory chips is the same "solution" to this employed by Small World, but it's a dodge, not a solution, since it relies on players being lazy and not simply counting the points their opponents are earning. The hidden objectives don't help much, as it seems pretty easy to obtain one's six bonus points, since no two players are ever competing for the same thing.

*Mix of tactical and strategic game play that rewards flexible planning

Cons:

*Lack of variety in event cards

RATING: 10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Another medieval worker placement game!

Scoops up all that's good from Alien Frontiers, and then dumps that in the [i]poubelle[/i], leaving you with an oh so slow dice-manipulation game.

Here's what it does have:

Hundred spinning Palissy plates. Check.
Obscure French naming of cards. Check.
Half-arsed icons. Check.
DRY gameplay. Grave dust dry. Gameplay so dry you'd better have a glass of Champagne handy. Check.
Vague theme. Check.
Many befuddling options. Check.
Dull board (not as dull as Conquest of Planet Earth, but pretty dull). Check.

Choosing what to do on your turns becomes angst-ridden, especially as everyone's victory points are kept secret from everyone else - resulting in 2 hours where no one is aware of the score, and no one feels particularly knowledgeable as to the best course of action to enable a win, and that's a large demerit. I can see clearly why the game doesn't have open scoring: analysis paralysis and number crunching, dragging the end game into infinity, but it still doesn't make me respect Troyes in the morning.

The game does have a couple of nice ideas, but they're hidden under a dusty old, moth-eaten medieval tapestry.

In the end, Troyes was not my cup of mead.

RATING: 7
This is a Euro Gamer's Euro game for sure.  The artwork is pleasant enough, but the opaqueness of the symbology and abstractness of the goals coupled with hard to read board positions make this feel more like a solo point maximization exercise than I expected (though certainly the dice your opponents use effect your plans regularly thorughout the game).  Really the first game I've played that felt like a collection of mechanics with no thematic feel.  I still really liked the game, but I doubt the "changing dice pips into points" game would see enough play in my group to be worth getting.

RATING: 8
Excellent, and delightfully unusual.  You know, this is the way to use dice.  This game is more of a brain-burner than you'd guess, because the way you hire people to get you dice, that get rolled, then used or hired out to secure positions of power, combat awful events, or otherwise wrangle for VP.  I wasn't completely sure how forcibly hiring each others' workforces (dice) away from each other would work out; although it does shift play a little toward the tactical end of the spectrum, there is still plenty of strategy to employ.  It can be a little challenging to think your way through the complexities of playing well, which is a wonderful thing.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Superb artwork on the cards that fits the theme. Easy to understand rules and good mechanisms. Very balanced. One of the best eurogames I played. It uses dice but there really is almost no luck in it. It does look a little bit dry with its gray board and that has turned people off, but when playing it you discover a solid well crafted game. 
I enjoy the different combo's that come out each game. The black cards that attack the town also make each game slightly different. 
What I miss is a little bit more interaction. You can push away meeples and buy up dice from other players, but still it feels a little bit like multiplayer solitaire. 
There is some AP because you don't know exactly which dice will still be available when it's your turn.

RATING: 6
With two players, it worked for me.
With four players, there are a couple problems:
a) downtime - you can not plan your move ahead because it depends on the available dice, and they can change a lot
b) catchup - if you fall behind on dice and/or roll badly, you're lost
Not quite clear to me why so many people seem to like it so much.
